Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a modern text on Quranic theory of knowledge. It first classifies types of guidance (hidaya) from the Quranic perspective: some are general for all creatures, some specific to humans; some are apparent like scriptures, some hidden like sensory guidance; there is guidance of talents (like instincts for good and evil), acquired guidance (like that of believers), scientific guidance (knowing God's pleasure through prayer direction and prayer acts), practical guidance (rites of Hajj and prayer movements), permanent guidance (like benefits), and temporary guidance (like miracles and dreams). The summary emphasizes that guidance is a source of knowledge, like light leading to God's decree, and that incomplete knowledge needs guidance. Then it transitions to education (ta'lim), explaining that in the Quran, it is divine direction, teaching what humans did not know. It is not achieved by ordinary mental transfer but by presential knowledge (ilm huduri), which is emanation or illumination. It is a divine honor for human effort. The highest means of knowledge is reading, as per the first revelation. The pen is a metaphor for all human knowledge. Education is like divine illumination for limited flashes, allowing understanding of difficult matters directly from God without intermediary. The passage distinguishes general education for all humans and special education for prophets, chosen ones, scholars, and righteous. It supports this with a quote from Alexis Carrel, who argues that scientific discoveries involve illumination and creative visualization beyond analysis. Carrel and Jacques Hadamard highlight the role of subconscious perceptions in scientific breakthroughs. The passage contrasts this illumination-based epistemology with purely rational or analytical methods.

هداية المواهب
هداية المكاسب

هداية عملية
هداية دائمة
هداية مؤقتة

Key Takeaway
Divine guidance is not monolithic; the Quranic theory presents a rich taxonomy of hidaya, each type addressing a different facet of human knowledge and life.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate

Divine Teaching (Ta'lim) as Presential Knowledge (Ilm Huduri)
Definition and explanation of divine teaching as presential knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conceptual knowledge (ilm husuli) and presential knowledge (ilm huduri) as presented in the Quranic concept of ta'lim, and explain its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
True education (ta'lim) in the Quran is a direct illumination from God, not mere mental transfer.

This lesson examines the Quranic notion of ta'lim (teaching) as a direct, presential knowledge from God. The passage contrasts this with ordinary mental transmission and describes it as an emanation or illumination. This idea challenges purely rationalistic epistemologies and emphasizes the role of divine grace in human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of ta'lim
The passage says ta'lim is 'تسديد من الله للانسان' (a direction from God) and equates it with 'علم حضوري' (presential knowledge).
Key Quranic verses: 'ويعلمكم ما لم تكونوا تعلمون' and 'وعلمكم الله'.
2
Understand the Quranic proof
The author cites verses to show that God teaches directly. Note that the teaching is not through intermediaries but a direct bestowal.
The passage emphasizes that this education is 'لا ينال بالنقل الذهني المتعارف' (not attained by customary mental transfer).
3
Contrast with customary mental transfer
What does 'النقل الذهني المتعارف' mean? It refers to ordinary learning through teachers, books, reasoning. The author claims ta'lim bypasses this.
This implies a form of knowledge that is immediate, like intuition or revelation.
4
Explore the metaphor of illumination
The passage describes it as 'كالضوء من سراج الغيب يقع على القلب' (like light from the lamp of the unseen falling upon the heart). Reflect on the immediacy and clarity implied.
This is reminiscent of the concept of 'ishraq' (illumination) in Islamic philosophy.
5
Apply to real knowledge
The passage quotes Alexis Carrel about scientific discoveries involving illumination beyond analysis. How does this support the idea of presential knowledge?
Think of sudden insights or inspirations in scientific or spiritual contexts.

The Tawqifiyya of Names: Quranic Evidence
Author presents the first example of divine teaching: God taught Adam all names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic evidence for the tawqifiyya of names and explain its epistemological significance for Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Names are divinely taught, not human conventions.

Where do names come from? Are they arbitrary labels invented by humans, or are they divinely appointed? This passage presents the first example of divine teaching: Allah taught Adam all the names. By examining the Quranic verses, we uncover a powerful argument that language itself is a gift from Allah, and that naming things without divine authority is blameworthy. This lesson will equip you to recognise the tawqifiyya argument and apply it to discussions in tafsir and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author asserts that names are tawqifiyya — divinely appointed. Find the statement in the Arabic that explicitly makes this claim.
2
Extract the Quranic evidence
Two Quranic verses are cited: the first (Al-Baqarah 2:31) describes Allah teaching Adam all names, and the second (An-Najm 53:23) condemns naming things without divine authority. Note how each verse functions as proof.
3
Understand the reasoning
The phrase 'then He presented them' (ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُم) is used as evidence that the names refer to the named entities (al-musammayat). Why does this phrase prove that names are for things?
The Arabic pronoun 'them' (هُم) refers to the named entities, not the names themselves.
4
Contrast with human convention
The second verse criticises those who assign names arbitrarily without divine sanction. What is the implication for the origin of language?
5
Apply to Islamic epistemology
Consider how this view of language affects how we understand terms in Islamic sciences. For example, if names are divinely taught, then the meanings of words like 'salat' or 'zakat' are not open to arbitrary redefinition.

التعليم العام لبني البشر : انموذجات من التعليم الالهي (۱) الله علم الاسماء : قال تعالى (وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ الْأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُم عَلَى الْمَلَائِكَة فَقَالَ أَنْبِئُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ إِن كُنتُمْ صَادِقِينَ) أي أسماء المسميات بدليل قوله (ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُم) فالاسماء توقيفية وان الله ذم قوما على تسميتهم الاشياء من عند انفسهم (إِنْ هِيَ إِلَّا أَسْمَاءٌ سَمَّيْتُمُوهَا أَنتُمْ وَآبَاؤُكُم مَّا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ بِهَا مِن سُلْطَانٍ)
The general teaching for humans: examples of divine teaching. (1) Allah taught the names: He said: 'And He taught Adam all the names, then He presented them to the angels and said: Inform Me of the names of these, if you are truthful.' That is, the names of the named things, as evidence by His saying 'then He presented them'. So names are divinely appointed (tawqifiyya), and Allah blamed a people for naming things from themselves: 'They are but names which you have named, you and your fathers, for which Allah has sent down no authority.'
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification. The author introduces the concept of general divine teaching for humans, then gives the first example: Allah teaching Adam all the names. The author explains that 'names' here refers to the names of the named entities (al-musammayat), citing the phrase 'then He presented them' — i.e., the names were presented to the angels, and they were asked to identify them. From this, the author derives that names are tawqifiyya, meaning they originate from divine appointment, not human convention. To reinforce this, the author quotes the verse condemning those who arbitrarily assign names without divine authority, as in the case of idol worship. The lesson is clear: the origin of language is a divine gift, not a human invention.

Key Terms
الأسماء
توقيفية

Divine Origin of Languages: Quranic Evidence and Traditional Account
Author presents the second example: Allah taught languages of the world, explaining their origin and diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic basis and traditional account for the divine origin of languages, and distinguish this from naturalistic theories.

Core Idea
Language originates from Allah, not from time or custom.

This passage presents the second example of divine teaching: languages. The author argues against naturalistic theories of language origin, citing the Quranic verse on diversity of tongues and colors. Then he recounts religious traditions that Allah taught Adam 72 languages, which are the roots of all languages, branching from four primary languages. We will analyze this account to understand the Islamic perspective on language origin and diversity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic evidence
Find the verse cited (Quran 30:22) and note how the author clarifies that 'tongues' (ألسنة) refers to languages, not physical tongues.
The verse is Surah al-Rum, verse 22.
2
Recognize the author's argument
Contrast divine teaching with naturalistic explanations (time or custom). The author states unequivocally that the beginning of language is from Allah's teaching.
3
Trace the chain of transmission
According to religious traditions, Allah taught Adam 72 languages, which are the origins of all languages. These languages stem from only four roots, from which all other languages branch.
The four roots are not specified here, but the concept of branching is emphasized.
4
Understand the role of human innovation
After the divine origin, humans invent new words and develop languages over time, leading to diversity across nations.
The author acknowledges human contribution while affirming divine origin.

۲ ) الله علم لغات العالم : وليس الزمن أو العادات (وَمِنْ آيَاتِهِ خَلْقُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَافُ أَلْسِنَتِكُمْ وَأَلْوَانِكُمْ إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَآيَاتٍ لِلْعَالِمِينَ) والمراد اختلاف اللغات وليس اختلاف الألسن. ابتداء اللغة وقع بالتعليم من الله سبحانه وتعالى والباقي يخترعه الانسان من نفسه وهذه اللغة تتشعب بين اقوام متباعدة وتتطور بما يحدث فيها التغيير والزيادة فتنبثق لغات خاصة وهي هذه الأسماء التي يتعارف بها الناس إنسان ودابة وأرض وسهل وبحر وجبل وحمار وأشباه ذلك من الأمم وغيرها . لا شك أن البشر متنوعون في الأذواق وفي البيئة وفي أنماط الحياة وبسبب ذلك تنوعت لغاتهم واختلفت وتقول الروايات الدينية أن الله تعالى علم آدم اثنتين وسبعين لغة وهي أصول لغات العالم، وهذه اللغات أصلها أربع فقط ومنها تتشعب بقية اللغات كلها حتى تصل إلى العدد الموجود اليوم.
(2) Allah taught the languages of the world: not time or customs. And among His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth and the diversity of your tongues and colors; indeed in that are signs for the worlds. The intended meaning is the diversity of languages, not tongues. The beginning of language occurred by teaching from Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, and the rest is invented by man from himself. This language branches among distant nations and develops with changes and additions, giving rise to specific languages - these names by which people recognize each other: human, animal, land, plain, sea, mountain, donkey, and similar things from nations and others. There is no doubt that humans are diverse in tastes, environment, and lifestyles, and due to that their languages diversified and differed. Religious traditions say that Allah taught Adam seventy-two languages, which are the origins of the world's languages, and these languages have only four roots, and from them branch all the remaining languages until reaching the number existing today.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the second example of divine teaching: Allah taught languages. The author contrasts it with naturalistic theories (time or custom). Citing the Quranic verse about diversity of tongues and colors, the author clarifies that 'tongues' (alsina) refers to languages, not the physical tongue. The initial origin of language is from Allah; subsequent innovations are human. Languages then branch and evolve into specific languages for different nations, giving examples like 'human, animal...' The author then notes that human diversity leads to linguistic diversity, and cites religious traditions that Allah taught Adam 72 languages as the roots, which then originate from four primary languages that branch into all existing ones. This underscores the divine origin of language while acknowledging human contribution and historical development.

Key Terms
اللغات (al-lughāt): Languages as distinct from physical tongues (alsina); the spoken systems of communication among different nations.
أصول اللغات (uṣūl al-lughāt): The seventy-two original languages taught to Adam, from which all other languages derive; reduced to four primary roots.

Key Takeaway

Bayan: The Divine Gift of Eloquent Expression
Author presents the third example: Allah taught the Quran and eloquence (bayan) to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bayan and balaghah as defined in the passage and explain why bayan is more fundamental than speech.

Core Idea
Bayan is Allah's gift of clear expression and comprehension, broader than speech.

The passage explores the Quranic verse on Allah teaching the Quran and bayan. It defines bayan as disclosure of meaning and places it above speech. The sayings of Imam Ali and Imam al-Sadiq further clarify that true eloquence (balaghah) is concise, accurate expression. In this lesson, we will unpack these definitions and see how they relate to the divine teaching of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic verse and its context
Locate the verse from Surah al-Rahman. Note the sequence: Allah taught the Quran, created man, then taught him bayan. This order indicates the priority of divine knowledge.
2
Extract the definition of bayan
Find the author's definition: 'al-bayan huwa al-kashf 'an al-shay'' (bayan is disclosure of something). Note it is more general than speech (a'amm min al-nutq).
3
Compare bayan to speech (natiq)
Analyze why bayan is broader. Speech is a means to express, but bayan also includes understanding others' expression. This shows bayan encompasses both production and comprehension.
4
Analyze the definitions of balaghah
Read the sayings from Imam Ali and Imam al-Sadiq. Identify common themes: hitting the meaning, conciseness, achieving the goal with ease. Contrast these with excessive speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۳) تعليم القرآن والبيان قال تعالى (الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَّمَ الْقُرآنَ خَلَقَ الْإِنْسَانَ عَلَّمَهُ الْبَيَانَ) علمه البيان علمه ان يبين ويعبر عما في ضميره وان يفهم بيان غيره والبيان هو الكشف عن الشيء وهو أعم من النطق الذي هو بدوره وسيلة اساسية من وسائل البيان وقد قدمت الآية الكريمة تعليم القرآن على خلق الانسان قال أصحاب النبي - ص - يارسول الله مالك أفصحنا لسانا وأبيننا بيانا ؟ قال : ان العربية أندرست فجاءني بها جبرائيل غضة طرية كما شق على لسان اسماعيل) وان اسماعيل الهم هذا اللسان العربي الهاما. قال الامام علي بن ابي طالب - ع - في غرر الحكم ( البلاغة ماسهل على المنطق وخف على الفطنة ) ( البلاغة ان تجيب فلا تبديء وتصيب فلا تخطيء ) ( أحسن الكلام مازانه حسن النظام . وفهمه الخاص والعام ) ( أحسن الكلام ما لا تمجه الاذان . ولا يتعب فهمه الاذهان . وعن الامام جعفر بن محمد الصادق - ع - في تحف العقول: ليست البلاغة بحدة اللسان ولا بكثرة الهذيان ولكنها بإصابة المعنى وقصد الحجة ) ( سئل الامام الصادق - ع - ما البلاغة ؟ فقال : من عرف شيئا قل كلامه فيه . وانما سمي البليغ لأنه يبلغ حاجته بأهون سعيه)
(3) Teaching of the Quran and Bayan. He said: 'The Most Merciful taught the Quran, created man, taught him eloquence.' He taught him eloquence: He taught him to clarify and express what is in his conscience and to understand the expression of others. Bayan is the disclosure of something, and it is more general than speech, which is itself a basic means of Bayan. The noble verse placed the teaching of the Quran before the creation of man. The companions of the Prophet – peace be upon him – said: 'O Messenger of Allah, why are you the most eloquent in tongue and clearest in expression?' He said: 'The Arabic language had become extinct, so Gabriel brought it to me fresh and tender as it was imparted to the tongue of Ishmael.' And Ishmael was inspired with this Arabic tongue as inspiration. Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib – peace be upon him – said in Ghurar al-Hikam: 'Eloquence is what is easy for logic and light for intelligence.' 'Eloquence is that you answer without delay and hit the mark without error.' 'The best speech is that adorned by good organization, and understood by the elite and the common.' 'The best speech is that which ears do not reject and minds do not find difficult to understand.' And from Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq – peace be upon him – in Tuhaf al-Uqul: 'Eloquence is not sharpness of tongue nor excessive rambling, but it is hitting the meaning and aiming at the proof.' 'He was asked: What is eloquence? He said: Whoever knows a thing, his speech about it becomes concise. The orator is called eloquent because he achieves his need with the least effort.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on divine teaching, focusing on the Quran and Bayan (eloquence/clear expression). The author begins with the Quranic verse from Surah al-Rahman, noting that Allah taught the Quran and then created man and taught him bayan. The author defines bayan as the ability to disclose and express inner thoughts and to understand others' expression, and it is broader than natiq (speech), which is a subset. The author highlights that the verse mentions teaching the Quran before creating man, indicating the primacy of divine knowledge. Then, a hadith is cited where the Prophet explains that Arabic was revived through Gabriel's teaching, as it was originally taught to Prophet Ishmael. Finally, several sayings from Imam Ali and Imam al-Sadiq on eloquence (balaghah) are quoted, defining it as concise, accurate, and effective communication that achieves its purpose easily. These sayings reinforce the idea that eloquence is a divine gift that enables clear and impactful expression.

البيان
البلاغة

Distinguishing Ta'lim and Hidaya as Quranic Sources of Knowledge
Author distinguishes ta'lim from hidaya and asserts ta'lim as a divine source of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'lim and hidaya as two distinct divine sources of knowledge, and identify examples of each in the Quranic worldview.

Core Idea
Ta'lim is divine inspiration for worldly sciences; hidaya is continuous guidance in creation and belief.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common confusion between two Quranic concepts: ta'lim (education) and hidaya (guidance). While both are divine in origin, they differ in scope, duration, and purpose. Understanding this distinction is essential for a proper Quranic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being contrasted
Locate the definitions of ta'lim and hidaya in the Arabic text. Note that the author explicitly begins the contrast with 'the difference between education (ta'lim) and guidance (hidaya) is...'
2
Analyze the criticism of those who deny divine emanation
The author quotes two poets and a hadith to criticize those who attribute inventions solely to themselves. This sets up the need for recognizing divine assistance (al-fayd al-ilahi) as a source of knowledge.
3
Extract the two categories of hidaya
Hidaya pertains to two matters: (1) guidance in matters of constitutional creation, which is temporally continuous; (2) guidance in matters of belief leading to success or loss.
4
Define ta'lim precisely
Ta'lim is divine inspiration (tasdid) for developing the sciences of life, such as industry, agriculture, and language. It is not continuous but occurs as spiritual illumination at moments concerning essential matters.
5
Synthesize the distinction using the conclusion
The author's conclusion (al-khulasa) summarizes that ta'lim is a spiritual illumination for essential matters and a source of knowledge in the Quranic theory, contrasting with the broader scope of hidaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن قسما من هؤلاء بقوا مكابرين فنسبوا هذه المخترعات الى أنفسهم وسكتوا عن الاشارة الى دور الفيض الالهي في معرفتهم. قال الشاعر على المرء ان يبذل قصارى جهده وليس عليه أن يكون موفقا وقال آخر (أنت الذي نال الكمال موفقاً ... من رازق من شاء غير محاسب) وقال كعب بن مالك يصف النبي محمد - ص - إذا كان منه القول كان موفقاً ... وإن كان حياً كان نوراً مجددا) ان الفرق بين التعليم والهداية ان الهداية تختص بأمرين هما هداية في امور اداء الخلقة التكوينية وهي مستمرة زمانيا طول حياة الكائن الحي وهداية في أمور الاعتقاد تقود صاحبها الى الفوز او الخسارة . أما التعليم فهو تسديد لتطوير علوم الحياة من صناعة وزراعة ولغة وغيرها . الخلاصة : ان التعليم هو تسديد الهي للانسان يمكن تمثيله بأشراقة روحية للحظات من الزمن تخص الامور الجوهرية ويعد واحدا من مصادر المعرفة في نظرية القرآن الكريم.
But a group among these remained obstinate, attributing these inventions to themselves and remaining silent about indicating the role of divine emanation in their knowledge. The poet said: 'Man must exert his utmost effort, but he is not required to be successful.' And another said: 'You who attained perfection successfully... from the Provider who gives to whom He wills without accounting.' And Ka'b bin Malik said describing the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'When he spoke, his speech was successful... and when he lived, he was a renewed light.' The difference between education (ta'lim) and guidance (hidaya) is that guidance pertains to two matters: guidance in matters of performing the constitutional creation, which is temporally continuous throughout the life of the living being, and guidance in matters of belief that lead its possessor to success or loss. As for education, it is divine inspiration for developing the sciences of life, such as industry, agriculture, language, and others. Conclusion: Education is a divine inspiration for man that can be represented as a spiritual illumination for moments of time concerning essential matters, and it is considered one of the sources of knowledge in the Quranic theory.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the sources of knowledge in the Quranic worldview. The author begins by criticizing a group of people who obstinately deny divine assistance (al-fayd al-ilahi) and attribute their inventions solely to themselves. He then cites poetic verses to emphasize that success is ultimately from God, not from human effort alone. Next, the author defines two key concepts: guidance (hidaya) and education (ta'lim). Hidaya is divided into two types: creational guidance, which sustains the living being throughout life, and belief guidance, which leads to success or loss in the afterlife. Ta'lim, in contrast, is described as divine inspiration (tasdid) for developing worldly sciences like industry and agriculture. The conclusion (al-khulasa) encapsulates that ta'lim is a spiritual illumination pertaining to essential matters and constitutes one source of knowledge according to the Quran. The passage thus establishes a hierarchy: hidaya is broader (continual and both creational and religious), while ta'lim is a specific divine gift for acquiring practical knowledge.

Key Terms
تعليم
هداية
فيض إلهي

Key Takeaway
Ta'lim and hidaya are distinct divine sources: hidaya is continuous creational and belief guidance, while ta'lim is occasional divine inspiration for developing practical sciences.

Dream Visions as a Source of Knowledge
Author introduces dream visions as a source of knowledge, with definitions and prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Islamic epistemological view of dream visions and identify the key distinctions made about the spirit during sleep.

Core Idea
Dream visions are a legitimate source of knowledge in Islam, but only the spirit of intellect leaves the body during sleep.

The author begins by examining various interpretations of sleep—physiological, Quranic, and metaphorical—to establish a foundation. He then cites narrations from the Imams to clarify that during sleep, the spirit of life remains while the spirit of intellect departs. Finally, he asserts that dream visions are a recognized source of knowledge about past, present, and future events, supported by Quranic verses and prophetic traditions. This lesson unpacks how these claims fit into Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of sleep
Find the three interpretations of sleep given: physiological (relaxation of brain nerves), Quranic (God taking the soul temporarily), and metaphorical (light death). Note that all are considered correct from different perspectives.
This shows the author's holistic approach.
2
Analyze the spirit's behavior during sleep
From the narration of Imam al-Sadiq, extract the key point: the spirit of life (ruh al-hayawan) remains in the body, while the spirit of intellect (ruh al-'aql) leaves. Contrast this with the common misunderstanding that the entire soul leaves.
The author interprets these as motive energy and thinking power.
3
Recognize the epistemological claim
Locate the statement that dream visions are a source of knowledge (manhal li-l-ma'rifa). Note that this is supported by Quranic verses, hadith, and narrations, and that dreams can inform about past, present, and future.
This establishes the epistemological legitimacy of dream visions.
4
Identify the incomplete listing of types
The passage begins to list cognitive dream visions starting with 'al-ru'ya al-khassa' (special dream vision) for prophets, citing Prophet Ibrahim's vision, but the text is cut off. Observe that the author intended to enumerate more types.
This incompleteness is noted in the source; further types are not provided.

الرؤيا المنامية النوم فسر على أوجه كلها صحيح بنظرات مختلفة : قيل هو استرخاء أعصاب الدماغ برطوبات البخار الصاعد إليه وقيل هو ان يتوفى الله النفس من غير موت اللهُ يَتَوَفَّى الْأَنْفُسَ حِينَ مَوتِهَا وَالَّتِي لَمْ تَمُتْ فِي مَنَامِهَا فَيُمْسِكَ الَّتِي قَضَى عَلَيْهَا الْمَوْتَ وَيُرْسِلُ الْأَخْرَى إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُسَمَّى (الله) وقيل النوم موت خفيف والموت نوم ثقيل ورجل نؤوم ونومة كثير النوم والمنام النوم قال ( ومن اياته منامكم باليل - وجعلنا نومكم سباتا - لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم ( والنومة أيضا خامل الذكر واستنام فلان الى كذا اطمأن إليه والمنامة : الثوب الذي ينام فيه ونامت السوق كسدت ونام الثوب أخلق أو خلق معا واستعمال النوم فيهما على التشبيه )) ( ورد عن الإمام الصادق (عليه السلام) ( سأله أبو بصير عن الروح عند النوم أخارج من الأبدان ؟ :قال: لا يا أبا بصير، فإن الروح إذا فارقت البدن لم تعد إليه، غير أنها بمنزلة عين الشمس مركوزة في السماء في كبدها وشعاعها في الدنيا روي عن أبي الحسن عليه السلام يقول : إن المرء إذا نام فإن روح الحيوان باقية في البدن ، والذى يخرج منه روح العقل) ولاشك ان المقصود بروح الحيوان هو الطاقة المحركة وروح العقل هو القوة المفكرة ان صح التعبير . وفي مصطلحات علم الكهرباء تسمى هذه العلاقة سيطرة بعيدة (رموت كونترول وردت آيات كريمة واحاديث شريفة للنبي محمد ( ص ) و أقوال لأهل بيت النبي وروايات في كتب الصحاح والسنن تقول ان الرؤيا المنامية منهل للمعرفة يغترف منه الانسان كثير من الاخبار الماضية والحاضرة والمستقبلية وتكون الرؤيا المعرفية على انواع عدة أهمها: ١ - الرؤيا المنامية الخاصة وردت شواهد عديدة في القرآن الكريم تبين ان الانبياء يتلقون بعض معارفهم خلال النوم ونذكر من هذه الآيات رؤيا نبي الله ابراهيم الخليل عليه
The dream vision (al-ru'ya al-manamiyya): Sleep has been interpreted in various ways, all correct from different perspectives: It is said to be the relaxation of the brain's nerves due to the moisture of the vapor rising to it, and it is said that God takes the soul without death: 'God takes the souls at the time of their death and those that do not die in their sleep; He withholds those for which He has decreed death and sends the others until an appointed term' (God). And it is said that sleep is a light death and death is a heavy sleep. A man is called nawum or nawma if he sleeps a lot. Al-manam is sleep. He said: 'And among His signs is your sleep by night' and 'We made your sleep a repose' and 'No drowsiness seizes Him nor sleep.' Al-nawma also means obscure in mention. Istinama fulan ila kadha means he felt reassured by it. Al-manama is the garment in which one sleeps. The market slept (i.e., was sluggish). The garment slept means it became worn out or wore out together, and the use of 'sleep' in these is metaphorical. It is related from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): Abu Basir asked him about the spirit during sleep: does it leave the body? He said: No, O Abu Basir, for if the spirit left the body it would not return to it; rather it is like the eye of the sun fixed in the sky in its orb, while its rays are on the earth. It is related from Abu al-Hasan (peace be upon him) that he said: When a person sleeps, the spirit of life remains in the body, and what leaves is the spirit of intellect. There is no doubt that the intended meaning of 'spirit of life' is the motive energy, and 'spirit of intellect' is the thinking power, if the expression is correct. In the terminology of electrical science, this relationship is called remote control. Noble verses and noble hadiths of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and sayings of the Ahl al-Bayt and narrations in the books of Sahih and Sunan state that the dream vision is a source of knowledge from which man draws much information about the past, present, and future. The cognitive dream vision is of several types, the most important of which are: 1 - The special dream vision. Numerous evidences have been mentioned in the Noble Quran indicating that prophets receive some of their knowledge during sleep, and among these verses is the vision of Prophet Ibrahim al-Khalil (peace be upon him)...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about dream visions (al-ru'ya al-manamiyya) as a source of knowledge within the Islamic tradition. It begins by defining sleep from multiple perspectives: physiological (relaxation of brain nerves), Quranic (God's taking of souls), and metaphorical (sleep as light death). Then it cites a narration from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) explaining that the spirit does not fully leave the body during sleep; rather, the spirit of life (ruh al-hayawan) remains, while the spirit of intellect (ruh al-'aql) departs. The author interprets these as motive energy and thinking power, respectively, and draws an analogy to remote control in modern electrical science. The passage then asserts that numerous Quranic verses, prophetic hadiths, and narrations from the Ahl al-Bayt affirm that dream visions are a source of knowledge about past, present, and future events. Finally, it begins to list types of cognitive dream visions, starting with the special dream vision (al-ru'ya al-manamiyya al-khassa) received by prophets, citing the example of Prophet Ibrahim's vision. The passage is cut off at this point, but it establishes dream visions as a legitimate epistemological source in the Islamic worldview.

True Dreams as a Source of Knowledge in Quranic Epistemology
Author explains the unconscious and true dreams as sources of knowledge according to the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic basis for treating true dreams as a valid source of knowledge and distinguish them from mere fantasies.

Core Idea
True dreams are a Quranically recognised source of knowledge beyond sense and reason.

In this passage, the author argues that the unconscious and true dreams are valid sources of knowledge. While modern thought often limits knowledge to sensory or rational sources, the Quran presents a broader epistemology. By understanding the nature of true dreams and their role in revelation and guidance, we expand our conception of how humans can know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources presented
The author lists the unconscious manifesting in consciousness and true dreams as sources. Note that each points to dimensions of the human person beyond surface appearance and beyond ordinary experience.
2
Define true dream from the passage
The true dream is defined as realistic images seen during sleep that report definite events unknown to the dreamer beforehand. This is a precise epistemological claim.
3
Analyse the epistemological claim
The author concludes that dream-sleep is one of the sources of knowledge according to the Quran. Consider why the Quran includes dreams: they provide information not obtainable through senses or reason.
4
Connect to Quranic examples
Recall the dreams of Prophet Yusuf (interpretation of dreams), the Prophet Muhammad's dreams that foretold events, and the hadith that 'the dream of the believer is one forty-sixth of prophethood'. These validate the claim that true dreams are a source of knowledge.

C - The true dream; what is meant by it are the realistic images seen during sleep, reporting definite events that occurred before the dream, or at the time of it, or after it, and there was nothing of them in the mind of the person before they materialized.
The conclusion: that dream-sleep is one of the sources of knowledge, as the theory of the Noble Quran says.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition listing two sources of knowledge: the unconscious and true dreams. The author first states that the unconscious manifesting in consciousness shows that human personality has both outward and inward aspects. He then defines true dreams as realistic images seen during sleep that accurately report definite events, unknown to the dreamer beforehand. The conclusion affirms that dreams are a valid source of knowledge according to the Quranic worldview.

The Heart as a Cognitive Faculty in the Quran
Author defines 'heart' lexically and argues it is a cognitive faculty in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic concept of the heart as a cognitive faculty and distinguish it from a purely physical organ.

Core Idea
In the Quran, the heart is the seat of cognition, emotion, and spiritual understanding.

The Quran frequently refers to the heart (qalb) in contexts of reasoning, understanding, and faith. This lesson unpacks the lexical roots of the term and the key verses that establish the heart as an epistemological tool, not merely a physical organ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical root
The term 'qalb' derives from the root Q-L-B, meaning to turn or flip. Notice how the author uses this to explain why the heart is named so: because of its constant change and fluctuation.
The root also appears in words like 'taqlib' (turning) and 'inqilab' (returning).
2
Trace Quranic usage for cognitive functions
The author cites verses where the heart is associated with 'aql (reason), fiqh (understanding), and dhikra (reminder). List these verses and note the cognitive verbs paired with 'heart' (qalb).
3
Distinguish physical from spiritual heart
The author asks whether the heart is the physical organ or a spiritual symbol. Observe how the Quranic description transcends mere biology, treating the heart as the locus of faith, reasoning, and moral change.
The Prophet's supplication for steadfastness of the heart (thabbit qalbi) underscores its spiritual dimension.
4
Recognize the heart's epistemological role
The author concludes that the heart is a 'locus of feeling, thinking, and understanding'. Reflect on how this challenges the modern separation of intellect and emotion.
In Islamic thought, the heart integrates cognition and affect.

(( قلب ؛ قلب الشيء ؛ تصريفه وصرفه عن وجه الى وجه كقلب الثوب وقلب الانسان أي صرفه عن طريقته قال ( وإليه تقلبون والانقلاب الانصراف قال ( إنقلبتم على أعقبكم ومن ينقلب على عقبيه ) وقلب الانسان قيل سمي به لكثرة تقلبه ويعبر عن القلب بالمعاني التي تختص به من العلم والشجاعة وغير ذلك وقوله (إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْبُ) أي علم وفهم وقوله (وَلَتَطْمَئِنُ بِهِ قُلُوبَكُمْ ( أي تثبت به شجاعتكم ويزول خوفكم وعلى عكسه (وَقَذَفَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الرُّعْب) وقوله (ذَلِكُمْ أَطْهَرُ لِقُلُوبِكُمْ وَقُلُوبِهِنَّ ( أي أجلب للعفة وقوله (وقلوبهم شتى ) أي متفرقة وتقليب الله القلوب والبصائر صرفها من رأي الى رأي قال (وَنُقَلْبُ أَفْئِدَتَهُم وأَبْصَارَهُمْ )

11 - The Heart
(( Heart; turning a thing; its manipulation and turning from one state to another, like turning a garment over, and turning a person means diverting him from his way. He said: 'And to Him you shall be turned back' and turning back is returning. He said: 'You turned back on your heels and whoever turns back on his heels.' The human heart is said to be named thus because of its frequent turning. And the heart is used to express meanings specific to it: knowledge, courage, and other things. His saying: 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' meaning knowledge and understanding. His saying: 'And to reassure your hearts thereby' meaning to confirm your courage and remove your fear, and its opposite: 'And He cast terror into their hearts.' His saying: 'That is purer for your hearts and their hearts' meaning more conducive to chastity. His saying: 'And their hearts are diverse' meaning scattered. And Allah's turning the hearts and insights means diverting them from one opinion to another. He said: 'And We turn their hearts and their insights.'
And the turning of the hand is an expression of regret, mentioning the state of the regretful person. He said: 'So he began to wring his hands' meaning he claps in regret. The poet said: 'Like one cheated who bites his hands, his loss becomes clear after the sale.'
With the intellect.
And 'taqallub' is manipulation. He said: 'And your turning among those who prostrate.' The heart represents the greatest advisor of the soul and the most important in making cognitive decisions. Often the heart dominates the intellect in many emotional decisions. So what is meant by the heart? He, Exalted, said: 'Have they not traveled through the earth so that they have hearts by which they reason?' and 'Surely We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind; they have hearts with which they do not understand.' and 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart or listens while witnessing.' and 'But Allah has endeared to you faith and adorned it in your hearts and made hateful to you disbelief, wickedness, and disobedience. Those are the rightly guided.' We conclude from the texts of the noble verses mentioned above that the heart, in the perspective of the Noble Quran, is a locus of feeling, thinking, and understanding. And with this meaning came the supplication of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' The concept of the heart has been presented as an important and essential cognitive tool in the Quranic theory. But we will ask the question again: What is the heart? Is what is meant the physical pineal muscular body that pumps blood to the body? Or is it meant as a reference to a spiritual heart as a symbol of deep organized thinking?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and lexical analysis of the term 'heart' (qalb). The author begins by giving the linguistic definition of 'qalb' as turning and flipping, then applies it to the human heart, noting it is named for its constant change. He then explains that the heart is metaphorically used for cognitive and emotional qualities like knowledge and courage, citing Quranic verses. The discussion moves to a list of Quranic verses that associate the heart with reason, understanding, and faith. The author concludes that, in the Quranic view, the heart is the seat of feeling, thinking, and comprehension. He references the Prophet's supplication for steadfastness. Finally, he poses a key question: is the heart the physical organ that pumps blood, or a spiritual symbol for deep organized thinking? This frames the heart as essential to the Quranic theory of knowledge.

Greed and Love of Dunya: Corrupting the Heart's Certitude
Analysis of greed's effect on the heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how greed (tama') and love of this world (dunya) undermine religious certitude (yaqin) as described in the hadith and poetry.

Core Idea
Greed seals the heart, destroying certitude.

In this passage, the author cites a powerful hadith warning against greed, which mixes avarice into the heart and seals it with love of this world. This directly leads to the weakening of religion and the corruption of certitude. Abu al-Atahiya's poetic line reinforces the idea that unmastered wealth enslaves its possessor. This lesson will help you dissect this cause-and-effect relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core warning
Find the hadith that begins 'إياكم واستشعار الطمع'. What does it warn against?
The hadith is a direct warning from the Prophet.
2
Analyze the effect on the heart
The hadith says greed 'يشوب القلب شدة الحرص ويختم على القلوب بطابع حب الدنيا'. Break down this phrase: what are the two effects?
One effect is mixing intense avarice; the other is sealing with love of this world.
3
Interpret the poem
Abu al-Atahiya's line: 'If a man does not free his soul from wealth, he is possessed by it.' How does this reinforce the hadith?
The poem shows that without self-liberation, wealth dominates the person.
4
Connect to certitude (yaqin)
The opening sentence says greed 'يوهن الدين ويفسد اليقين'. Explain the link between greed and loss of certitude.
Greed attaches the heart to the temporal, weakening conviction in the divine.

الليل والنهار وهذا ما يؤثر في طبيعة القرار الذي يتخذه القلب ويرفعه الى النفس قال تعالى (وَإِنْ عَلَيْكُمْ لَحَافِظِينَ كِرَامًا كَاتِبِينَ . يَعْلَمُونَ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ ) وفي مضمار الترهيب قال تعالى (وَالَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا وَكَذَّبُوا بِآيَاتِنَا أُولَئِكَ أَصْحَابُ النَّارِ هُم فِيهَا خَالِدُونَ ) وفي الترغيب قال تعالى (وَعَدَ اللهُ الْمُؤمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤمِنَاتِ جَنَّاتِ تَجْرِي مِنْ تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا وَمَسَاكِنَ طَيِّبَةً فِي جَنَّاتِ عَدْنٍ وَرِضْوَان مِنَ الله أكبرُ ذَلِكَ هُوَ الْفَوْزُ الْعَظِيمُ ) قال ابو تمام وكأنه يصف القلب (يا حساماً مهنداً وغماماً ... ديمتاه الترغيب والترهيب). حركات القلب وسكناته : روي عن النبي محمد (ص ) انه قال ( جبلت القلوب على حب من أحسن اليها وبغض من اساء اليها ) وعن تاثير القلب المعنوي على القلب المادي قال له ) إذا طاب قلب المرء طاب جسده واذا خبث القلب خبث الجسد ) ( ان الله تعالى في الارض اواني الا وهي القلوب فاحبها الى الله ارقها واصفاها واصلبها ارقها للاخوان واصفاها من الذنوب واصلبها في ذات الله ) ( سئل النبي الاكرم ما القلب السليم ؟ فقال: دين بلا شك وهوى وعمل بلا سمعة ولا رياء ) ( جلاء هذه القلوب ذكر الله وتلاوة القرآن وعن الامام علي عالم ) إذا شككت في مودة انسان فاسأل قلبك عنه) (ماجفت الدموع إلا لقسوة القلب وما قست القلوب إلا لكثرة الذنوب ) وفي غرر الحكم (معاشرة ذوي الفضائل حياة القلوب) (الحكمة شجرة تنبت في القلب وتثمر على اللسان) ( القلب مصحف البصر وروي عن الامام الصادق (ع ) قوله إن القلب يتلجلج في الجوف يطلب الحق فاذا اصابه اطمأن وقر ثم قرأ قوله تعالى ( فمن يرد الله ان يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام ومن يرد ان يضله يجعل صدره ضيقا حرجا كأنما يصعد في السماء ) الله فلا تسكن حرم الله غير الله (الغضب ممحقة لقلب القلب حرم الحكيم ومن لم يملك غضبه لم يملك عقله
It weakens religion and corrupts certitude, as mentioned in the hadith: 'Beware of feeling greed, for it mixes into the heart intense avarice and seals the hearts with the stamp of love for this world.' Abu al-Atahiya said: 'If a man does not free his soul from wealth, he is possessed by the wealth that possesses him.' 3) Intimidation and Enticement: Among the factors influencing the decisions of the heart are intimidation by the Fire and punishment, and enticement by Paradise and its pleasures. In the Quranic theory, every human has guardians from the angels recording his good and evil deeds and words night and day, and this affects the nature of the decision that the heart makes and raises to the soul. Allah said: 'And indeed, over you are keepers, noble writers. They know whatever you do.' In the context of intimidation, Allah said: 'But those who disbelieve and deny Our signs, those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein eternally.' And in enticement, Allah said: 'Allah has promised the believing men and believing women gardens beneath which rivers flow, wherein they abide eternally, and pleasant dwellings in gardens of perpetual residence; but approval from Allah is greater. That is the great attainment.' Abu Tammam said, as if describing the heart: 'O polished sword and raincloud, whose two rains are enticement and intimidation.' Movements and stillnesses of the heart: It is narrated from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Hearts are naturally disposed to love those who do good to them and hate those who do evil to them.' Regarding the influence of the spiritual heart over the physical heart, he said to him: 'When the heart of a person is good, his body is good; and when the heart is corrupted, the body is corrupted.' 'Indeed, on earth there are vessels, and they are the hearts. The most beloved to Allah are the most tender, purest, and firmest: the most tender toward brothers, the purest from sins, and the firmest in the cause of Allah.' The Noble Prophet was asked: 'What is the sound heart?' He said: 'Religion without doubt and desire, and action without reputation or hypocrisy.' 'The polish of these hearts is remembrance of Allah and recitation of the Quran.' Imam Ali (peace be upon him) said: 'If you doubt a person's friendship, ask your heart about him.' 'Tears do not flow except from hardness of the heart, and hearts are not hardened except from abundance of sins.' In Ghurar al-Hikam: 'Association with virtuous people is life for hearts.' 'Wisdom is a tree that grows in the heart and bears fruit on the tongue.' 'The heart is the copybook of the eye.' It is narrated from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The heart wobbles in the chest seeking truth, and when it finds it, it becomes calm and settled.' Then he recited the verse of Allah: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his chest to Islam; and whoever He wants to mislead, He makes his chest tight and constricted as if he were climbing into the sky.' 'Do not settle in the sanctuary of Allah other than Allah.' 'Anger is destructive to the heart.' 'The heart is the sanctuary of the Wise; whoever does not control his anger does not control his intellect.'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the factors that influence the heart's decisions and certitude (yaqin). The author begins by citing a hadith warning against greed (tama'), which corrupts the heart and seals it with love of this world, thus weakening religion and destroying certitude. A poem by Abu al-Atahiya reinforces the idea that a person enslaved by wealth loses self-control. The author then introduces two major factors: tarhib (intimidation by punishment) and targhib (enticement by reward), which affect the heart's decisions. He supports this with Quranic verses about recording angels (Surah al-Infitar) and the ultimate outcomes for believers and disbelievers (Surah al-Ra'd, Surah al-Tawbah). A line from Abu Tammam poetically depicts the heart as being rained upon by these two influences. The latter part of the passage deals with the movements and states of the heart (qalb) as described in hadith. The heart is naturally inclined to love benefactors and hate wrongdoers. There is a connection between the spiritual heart and the physical body: a pure heart leads to a sound body. The heart is described as a vessel; the best are tender, pure, and firm. The sound heart (qalb salim) is defined as having religion free from doubt and desire, and actions free from show and hypocrisy. Purification of the heart occurs through remembrance of Allah and recitation of the Quran. Imam Ali advises consulting one's heart about friendships, indicating the heart's intuitive knowledge. Hardness of heart results from sins, and good company brings life to the heart. Wisdom originates in the heart and is expressed on the tongue. The heart is like a scribe for the eye, recording what it sees. A hadith from Imam al-Sadiq describes the heart's restlessness until it finds truth, then settles, echoing the Quranic verse about expansion of the chest for Islam (Surah al-An'am). The passage concludes with warnings against settling for anything other than Allah and the destructive effect of anger on the heart and intellect.

Key Terms
القلب
الطمع
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the hadith say about greed?
A) It strengthens iman.
B) It seals the heart with love of dunya.
C) It increases knowledge.
D) It purifies the heart.

Key Takeaway
Greed is a spiritual poison that directly undermines certitude by anchoring the heart to this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List three ways the hadith describes greed's effect on the heart, and explain how each leads to loss of certitude.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

How Tarhib and Targhib Influence the Heart's Decisions
Examination of tarhib and targhib as motivational factors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the Quranic concepts of tarhib (intimidation) and targhib (enticement) are presented as factors influencing the heart's decisions.

Core Idea
The heart's decisions are shaped by the fear of punishment and hope for reward.

The author introduces tarhib (intimidation by the Fire) and targhib (enticement by Paradise) as key factors affecting the heart's decisions. The Quranic theory of recording angels amplifies this influence. This lesson will show you how knowledge of divine accountability sways the heart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key factors
Locate where the author explicitly names tarhib and targhib as influential factors on the heart's decisions.
They are introduced together at the start of the second section.
2
Find the Quranic evidence for recording angels
The passage mentions that every human has guardian angels recording deeds night and day. Find the verse cited as evidence.
The verse is from Surah al-Infitar: 'And indeed, over you are keepers, noble writers.'
3
Locate the specific verses for tarhib and targhib
Identify the verse used for intimidation (tarhib) and the one for enticement (targhib).
Tarhib verse: 'Those who disbelieve... are companions of the Fire.' Targhib verse: 'Allah has promised believing men and women gardens...'
4
Explain the mechanism of influence
How does knowing about recording angels and the ultimate outcomes affect the heart's decision-making?
The heart considers consequences before acting, leaning toward behaviors that lead to reward and away from those that lead to punishment.

The Sound Heart (Qalb Salim) and Its Purification
Attributes and purification of the sound heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the attributes of the sound heart (al-qalb al-salim) and the means of its purification as described in the hadiths.

Core Idea
The sound heart is free from doubt and hypocrisy, purified through dhikr and Quran.

The passage concludes with a series of hadiths describing the nature of the heart and the means to purify it. The sound heart (qalb salim) is defined as religion without doubt or desire, and action without show or hypocrisy. This lesson will help you identify its attributes and the methods to attain it.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of the sound heart
Locate the hadith where the Prophet is asked about the sound heart. What is his answer?
It is: 'Religion without doubt and desire, and action without reputation or hypocrisy.'
2
Identify the components of the definition
Break down the two parts: what goes into belief (religion) and what goes into action?
Belief must be free from doubt and desire; action must be free from seeking fame and hypocrisy.
3
List the means of purification
The passage mentions specific practices that polish the heart. What are they?
Remembrance of Allah (dhikr) and recitation of the Quran.
4
Note additional characteristics
Other hadiths describe the heart's natural dispositions and signs of health. What are some of those?
Hearts naturally love those who do good; the best hearts are tender, pure, and firm; tears flow from hardness; good company gives life.
5
Understand the consequence of a hardened heart
What leads to hardness of heart according to the hadith?
Abundance of sins hardens the heart, and anger is destructive to it.

السليم ؟ فقال: دين بلا شك وهوى وعمل بلا سمعة ولا رياء ) ( جلاء هذه القلوب ذكر الله وتلاوة القرآن وعن الامام علي عالم ) إذا شككت في مودة انسان فاسأل قلبك عنه) (ماجفت الدموع إلا لقسوة القلب وما قست الحق فاذا اصابه اطمأن وقر ثم قرأ قوله تعالى ( فمن يرد الله ان يهديه يشرح صدره للإسلام ومن يرد ان يضله يجعل صدره ضيقا حرجا كأنما يصعد في السماء ) الله فلا تسكن حرم الله غير الله (الغضب ممحقة لقلب القلب حرم الحكيم ومن لم يملك غضبه لم يملك عقله
It weakens religion and corrupts certitude, as mentioned in the hadith: 'Beware of feeling greed, for it mixes into the heart intense avarice and seals the hearts with the stamp of love for this world.' Abu al-Atahiya said: 'If a man does not free his soul from wealth, he is possessed by the wealth that possesses him.' 3) Intimidation and Enticement: Among the factors influencing the decisions of the heart are intimidation by the Fire and punishment, and enticement by Paradise and its pleasures. In the Quranic theory, every human has guardians from the angels recording his good and evil deeds and words night and day, and this affects the nature of the decision that the heart makes and raises to the soul. Allah said: 'And indeed, over you are keepers, noble writers. They know whatever you do.' In the context of intimidation, Allah said: 'But those who disbelieve and deny Our signs, those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein eternally.' And in enticement, Allah said: 'Allah has promised the believing men and believing women gardens beneath which rivers flow, wherein they abide eternally, and pleasant dwellings in gardens of perpetual residence; but approval from Allah is greater. That is the great attainment.' Abu Tammam said, as if describing the heart: 'O polished sword and raincloud, whose two rains are enticement and intimidation.' Movements and stillnesses of the heart: It is narrated from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Hearts are naturally disposed to love those who do good to them and hate those who do evil to them.' Regarding the influence of the spiritual heart over the physical heart, he said to him: 'When the heart of a person is good, his body is good; and when the heart is corrupted, the body is corrupted.' 'Indeed, on earth there are vessels, and they are the hearts. The most beloved to Allah are the most tender, purest, and firmest: the most tender toward brothers, the purest from sins, and the firmest in the cause of Allah.' The Noble Prophet was asked: 'What is the sound heart?' He said: 'Religion without doubt and desire, and action without reputation or hypocrisy.' 'The polish of these hearts is remembrance of Allah and recitation of the Quran.' Imam Ali (peace be upon him) said: 'If you doubt a person's friendship, ask your heart about him.' 'Tears do not flow except from hardness of the heart, and hearts are not hardened except from abundance of sins.' In Ghurar al-Hikam: 'Association with virtuous people is life for hearts.' 'Wisdom is a tree that grows in the heart and bears fruit on the tongue.' 'The heart is the copybook of the eye.' It is narrated from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The heart wobbles in the chest seeking truth, and when it finds it, it becomes calm and settled.' Then he recited the verse of Allah: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his chest to Islam; and whoever He wants to mislead, He makes his chest tight and constricted as if he were climbing into the sky.' 'Do not settle in the sanctuary of Allah other than Allah.' 'Anger is destructive to the heart.' 'The heart is the sanctuary of the Wise; whoever does not control his anger does not control his intellect.'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the factors that influence the heart's decisions and certitude (yaqin). The author begins by citing a hadith warning against greed (tama'), which corrupts the heart and seals it with love of this world, thus weakening religion and destroying certitude. A poem by Abu al-Atahiya reinforces the idea that a person enslaved by wealth loses self-control. The author then introduces two major factors: tarhib (intimidation by punishment) and targhib (enticement by reward), which affect the heart's decisions. He supports this with Quranic verses about recording angels (Surah al-Infitar) and the ultimate outcomes for believers and disbelievers (Surah al-Ra'd, Surah al-Tawbah). A line from Abu Tammam poetically depicts the heart as being rained upon by these two influences. The latter part of the passage deals with the movements and states of the heart (qalb) as described in hadith. The heart is naturally inclined to love benefactors and hate wrongdoers. There is a connection between the spiritual heart and the physical body: a pure heart leads to a sound body. The heart is described as a vessel; the best are tender, pure, and firm. The sound heart (qalb salim) is defined as having religion free from doubt and desire, and actions free from show and hypocrisy. Purification of the heart occurs through remembrance of Allah and recitation of the Quran. Imam Ali advises consulting one's heart about friendships, indicating the heart's intuitive knowledge. Hardness of heart results from sins, and good company brings life to the heart. Wisdom originates in the heart and is expressed on the tongue. The heart is like a scribe for the eye, recording what it sees. A hadith from Imam al-Sadiq describes the heart's restlessness until it finds truth, then settles, echoing the Quranic verse about expansion of the chest for Islam (Surah al-An'am). The passage concludes with warnings against settling for anything other than Allah and the destructive effect of anger on the heart and intellect.

الذكر
القرآن
الإخلاص

The Quranic Metaphor of Sharh al-Sadr
Author defines sharh al-sadr as expansion and contrasts it with constriction using Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the literal and metaphorical meanings of sharh al-sadr and explain its role as an epistemological condition for receiving divine guidance.

Core Idea
Expansion of the chest is the divine opening to faith, essential for acquiring true knowledge.

This lesson explores the Quranic concept of 'sharh al-sadr' (expansion of the chest) as a metaphor for receptivity to faith and knowledge. The author contrasts it with 'dayyiq' (constriction) to show how the heart's state determines one's ability to accept truth. Understanding this metaphor is crucial for appreciating the epistemological dimension of faith in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical meaning of sharh
The author defines 'al-sharh' as 'al-bast aw al-tawsi'a' (spreading or widening). Note that this is a physical term used metaphorically.
In logic, 'sharh' means commentary; here it is literal.
2
Locate the Quranic verses illustrating the metaphor
Find the verses cited: Surah al-Inshirah (94:1-2), al-An'am (6:125), al-Zumar (39:22), and others. Observe how they pair expansion with guidance and constriction with misguidance.
3
Analyze the contrast between expansion and constriction
The author uses the analogy of ascending in the sky (6:125) to depict the pressure of disbelief. This shows that constriction is not just emotional but physical, aligning with scientific knowledge.
4
Determine the purpose of expansion
The passage states: 'إن توسعة الصدر هدفها في منظور القرآن تهيئة الصدر لاستقبال نور الإيمان' (expansion aims to prepare the chest to receive the light of faith). This is the core epistemological function.
5
Distinguish material from spiritual expansion
The author notes that expansion can be material (physical sensation at altitude) or spiritual (removing grief). The spiritual type is more significant for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرح بمعنى البسط أو التوسعة ( أَلَمْ نَشْرَحْ لَكَ صَدْرَكَ ، وَوَضَعْنَا عَنْكَ وِزْرَكَ) ۲ ويقابل الشرح الضيق والحرج قال تعالى يخاطب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم (الَّذِينَ يَجْعَلُونَ مَعَ اللهِ إِلَهَا آخَرَ فَسَوفَ يَعْلَمُونَ . وَلَقَدْ نَعْلَمُ أَنَّكَ يَضِيقُ صَدْرُكَ بِمَا يقُولُونَ) وفي آية كريمة جمع الضيق و الشرح (فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَام وَمَن يُرِدْ أنْ يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا كَأَنَّمَا يصعد في السماء )٤ فقد صورت الآية ضيق الكافر بالايمان بقوة ضاغطة على الصدر بتصوير دقيق جدا يتفق مع نظريات العلم التي تؤكد انه كلما ارتفع الانسان في السماء ازداد الضغط كثافة واثرا على الصدر . ان توسعة الصدر هدفها في منظور القرآن تهيئة الصدر لاستقبال نور الايمان (أَفَمَنْ شَرَحَ اللهُ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَام فَهُوَ عَلَى نُورٍ مِنْ رَبِّهِ ) وفي دعاء نبي الله موسى لربه (قَالَ رَبِّ اشْرَحْ لِي صَدْرِي . ويسر لي أمري . واحْلُلْ عُقْدَةً مِنْ لِسَانِي ، يَفْقَهُوا قولي) ٦ (قَالَ رَبِّ إِنِّي أَخَافُ أَن يُكَذِّبُونِ ، ويَضِيقُ صَدْرِي وَلَا يَنطَلِقُ لساني لاحظوا ان ضيق الصدر او عدم انشراحه يمنع الانسان من الكلام وعندما يبالغ الكفار في ايذاء الرسول الله يخاطبه ربه (فَلَعَلَّكَ تَارِكٌ بَعْضَ مَا يُوحَى إِلَيْكَ وَضَائِقَ به صَدْرُكَ أَنْ يَقُولُوا لَوْلَا أُنْزِلَ عَلَيْهِ كَنزٌ أَوْ جَاءَ مَعَهُ مَلَكَ) وفي آية واحدة فقط في القرآن الكريم استخدم مفهوم شرح الصدر مع الكفر كنوع من الاستدراج والتهكم والانتقام وَلَكِنْ مَنْ شَرَحَ بِالْكُفْرِ صَدْرًا فَعَلَيْهِمْ غَضَبْ مِنَ اللهِ وَلَهُمْ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمٌ) والانشراح مرة يراد منه التوسعة المادية للصدر كقوله تعالى (فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ للْإِسْلَام وَمَن يُرِدْ أنْ يُصْلَهُ يَجْعَل صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا كَأَنْمَا يَصْعَدُ فِي السَّمَاءِ ) ومرة يراد منه الانشراح المعنوي باخراج الكمد والغيض وازالة الهموم والعقد النفسية (يَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ قَدْ جَاءَتْكُم مَوعِظَةً مِنْ رَبِّكُمْ وَشِفَاءٌ لِمَا فِي الصُّدُورِ ) ۳
Expansion means spreading or widening. (Did We not expand your breast for you, and relieve you of your burden?) And opposed to expansion is tightness and constriction. He, the Most High, said, addressing the Prophet, peace be upon him: (Those who set up another god with Allah will soon know. And indeed We know that your breast is straitened by what they say.) And in a noble verse, both tightness and expansion are mentioned: (So whoever Allah wills to guide, He expands his breast for Islam; and whoever He wills to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted, as if he were climbing up to the sky.) So the verse depicted the tightness of the disbeliever towards faith with a powerful pressure on the chest, in a very precise portrayal that agrees with scientific theories which affirm that the higher a person ascends in the sky, the more dense and impactful the pressure becomes on the chest. The purpose of expanding the chest in the perspective of the Quran is to prepare the chest to receive the light of faith: (Is he whose breast Allah has expanded for Islam so that he is upon a light from his Lord?) And in the supplication of the Prophet Moses, peace be upon him: (He said: My Lord, expand my breast for me. And ease my affair for me. And untie the knot from my tongue, that they may understand my speech.) (He said: My Lord, indeed I fear that they will deny me, and my breast will be straitened, and my tongue will not be free.) Notice that tightness of the chest or its lack of expansion prevents a person from speaking. And when the disbelievers go to extremes in harming the Messenger, Allah addresses him: (Then perhaps you would leave out some of what is revealed to you, and your breast is straitened by it, because they say, 'Why has a treasure not been sent down upon him, or an angel come with him?') And in only one verse in the Noble Quran was the concept of expansion of the chest used with disbelief as a kind of gradual enticement, mockery, and vengeance: (But whoever expands his breast with disbelief, upon them is wrath from Allah, and for them is a great punishment.) Expansion sometimes intends the material widening of the chest, as in His saying: (So whoever Allah wills to guide, He expands his breast for Islam; and whoever He wills to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted, as if he were climbing up to the sky.) And sometimes it intends spiritual expansion by removing grief, anger, and eliminating worries and psychological complexes: (O mankind, there has come to you an admonition from your Lord and a healing for what is in the breasts.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage from the book 'Nazariyya Falsafa' explains the concept of 'شرح الصدر' (expansion of the chest) as a Quranic metaphor for openness and receptivity to faith, contrasting it with 'ضيق الصدر' (tightness of the chest) which signifies resistance or distress. The author begins by defining 'الشرح' lexically as spreading or widening ('البسط أو التوسعة'), then immediately cites Quranic verses that demonstrate the term's usage, particularly Surah al-Inshirah (94:1-2) and then Surah al-Hijr (15:96-97) where the Prophet's chest tightens due to the polytheists' speech. A key verse cited is Surah al-An'am (6:125), which juxtaposes the two states: guidance leads to expansion, misguidance to constriction likened to ascending into the sky—an analogy the author notes aligns with scientific understanding of atmospheric pressure increasing with altitude. The author then explains the purpose of expansion: to prepare the heart for the light of faith, quoting Surah al-Zumar (39:22). The story of Prophet Moses is invoked, where he prays for expansion of his chest to facilitate speech and mission, illustrating that constriction hinders expression. The author notes that even the Prophet Muhammad experienced tightness due to the disbelievers' accusations, referenced in Surah Hud (11:12). A unique verse (Surah al-Nahl, 16:106) is mentioned where expansion is used negatively for those who embrace disbelief, indicating divine wrath. Finally, the author distinguishes between material expansion (physical sensation at altitude) and spiritual expansion (removing grief and psychological burdens), citing Surah Yunus (10:57) that the Quran heals what is in the breasts. Throughout, the passage emphasizes the emotional and cognitive dimension of 'شرح الصدر' as a prerequisite for receiving knowledge (faith) and performing tasks effectively.

الشرح
الصدر
الضيق

The Quranic Concept of Sadr as a Repository of Knowledge
Author defines 'sadr' as the seat of knowledge, emotions, and spiritual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic definition of 'sadr' as a repository of knowledge and evaluate the author's use of empirical evidence to support it.

Core Idea
The chest (sadr) stores information, emotions, and wills – not just blood.

The Quran describes the chest as the seat of inner states like faith and doubt. In this passage, the author builds a comprehensive definition by combining sacred texts, poetry, and modern organ transplant cases. Let's follow his argument step by step to understand how he arrives at the conclusion that the chest is a storehouse for much more than the physical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept being defined
Locate the Arabic term 'sadr' and list all the things the author claims it stores: information, whispers, sayings, actions, wills, and thoughts.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the quoted verses (Yunus 10:57, Tawba 9:14) and explain how they support the idea that the chest is affected by spiritual and emotional experiences like admonition and victory.
3
Analyze the empirical arguments
Summarize the two transplant cases (lungs and heart). What evidence do they provide according to the author? List the specific claims about memory transfer.
The author uses these as empirical confirmation of the Quranic view.
4
Evaluate the inductive leap
Assess whether the transplant cases logically prove that the chest stores memories. Identify potential alternative explanations (e.g., psychological suggestion, coincidence).
5
Synthesize the definition
Restate the author's final definition of 'sadr' in your own words. Note that it is both a physical and epistemic concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان الشرح والضيق أو البسط والانقباض حالتان خطرتان في حياة الانسان لهما تاثير على سعادة الانسان او شقائه وفي شفاء الصدر او مرضه فالموعظة مثلا تشفي الصدور والنصر على الاعداء شفاء لما في الصدور لانه يرفع الهم والغم والكمد (قَاتِلُوهُم يعذبهم الله بأيديكم ويُخْزِهِم وينصركُم عَلَيْهِم ويشف صدور قوم مؤمنين). قال حاتم الطائي : أمَاوي ما يُغني الثراء عن الفتى إذا حشرجت يوماً وضاق بها الصدر) إذا ازدحمت هموم الصدر قلنا ... عسى يوما يكون لها انفراج. لقد ثبت من خلال التجارب الطبية التي اجريت في الولايات المتحدة الامريكية والتي تم خلالها استبدال رئتين مريضتين لاحد الاشخاص برئتين سليمتين من شخص آخر تعرض للموت بحادث دهس حيث انتزعت رئتيه قبل موته ان الشخص الذي زرعت فيه الرئتان اخذ يتذكر معلومات خاصة بالميت المدهوس. وكذلك حدث الامر نفسه عند استبدال زراعة قلب من شخص لآخر بالطريقة نفسها ان الشخص الذي زرع له قلب سليم اخذ يتذكر قصائد رومانسية تعود للميت صاحب القلب المزروع بمجرد ان تقرأ الام البيت الاول من قصائد وجدتها في خزانة ولدها الميت ونصل من خلال الحقائق القرآنية التي ايدتها التجارب الطبية الحديثة الى القول الجازم وهو ان الطب لو تطور الى درج خلع رأس رجل وزراعته في جسد جديد لتغير تفكير الرأس البشري بشكل جوهري بتأثير القلب والرئتين والتصميم الفطري للجسد الجديد وكذلك اذا تم استبدال الرئتين أو استبدال القلب . والخلاصة : ان الصدر في نظرية القرآن مكمن ومخزن لكثير من المعلومات والوساوس والأقوال والأفعال والإرادات والأفكار.
Expansion and constriction, or openness and contraction, are two critical states in human life, having an effect on man's happiness or misery, and on the healing of the chest or its illness. For example, admonition heals the chests, and victory over enemies is healing for what is in the chests because it lifts worry, grief, and distress. 'Fight them; Allah will punish them by your hands and disgrace them and give you victory over them and satisfy the breasts of a believing people.' Hatim al-Tai said: 'O Umawi, wealth does not avail the youth when one day he gasps and his chest is constrained.' When the worries of the chest crowd, we say: 'Perhaps one day there will be relief for it.' It has been established through medical experiments conducted in the United States of America, during which two diseased lungs of a person were replaced with two healthy lungs from another person who died in a hit-and-run accident, where his lungs were removed before his death, that the person in whom the lungs were transplanted began to recall specific information about the deceased. Likewise, the same thing happened when a heart was transplanted from one person to another in the same manner: the person who had a healthy heart transplanted began to recall romantic poems belonging to the deceased heart donor as soon as the mother read the first line of poems she found in her deceased son's closet. We conclude, through the Quranic truths supported by modern medical experiments, the definitive statement that if medicine advances to the point of removing a man's head and transplanting it into a new body, the thinking of the human head would fundamentally change due to the influence of the heart, lungs, and the innate design of the new body, and likewise if the lungs or the heart are replaced. The conclusion: The chest, in the Quranic theory, is a repository and storehouse for much information, whispers, sayings, actions, wills, and thoughts.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition defining the concept of 'sadr' (chest) as the central locus of human knowledge, emotions, and spiritual states. The author begins by describing expansion and constriction as two critical states affecting human happiness and health. He cites Quranic verses showing that admonition heals chests (e.g., Yunus 10:57) and victory over enemies brings healing to chests (Tawba 9:14). He then quotes pre-Islamic poet Hatim al-Tai to illustrate constriction. The author then presents modern medical experiments—lung and heart transplants—which allegedly show recipients acquiring memories of donors. He uses these to argue that the chest (including heart and lungs) is a repository of information and that even a head transplant would transfer thinking due to the influence of these organs. The final summary states that in Quranic theory, the chest stores information, whispers, actions, wills, and thoughts. The author is unifying Quranic description with empirical evidence to assert a material basis for the chest's epistemological role.

Key Terms
صدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the chest (sadr) in Quranic theory?
A) It is merely a physical organ for breathing.
B) It is a repository for information, thoughts, emotions, and wills.
C) It only stores memories of previous lives.
D) It has no significant epistemological function.

Key Takeaway
In Quranic theory, the chest (sadr) is a comprehensive storehouse for both material and immaterial human content.

The Sexual Instinct's Influence on Knowledge and Decision-Making
Author argues sexual instinct affects knowledge and decisions, gives an example from Salamah Musa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the sexual instinct's role in influencing decisions from its role in producing knowledge, based on the author's argument.

Core Idea
The sexual instinct does not create knowledge, but it powerfully steers decisions.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial epistemological point: while the sexual instinct does not generate knowledge itself, it exerts a profound influence on human decision-making and behavior. Understanding this distinction is essential for recognizing how non-rational factors can affect our cognitive processes. We will examine the author's argument, his warning about sexual repression as a satanic entry point, and a real-world incident that illustrates the potentially devastating effects of this instinct when unmanaged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author's Main Claim
Read the first sentence carefully. The author asserts that the sexual instinct has a profound effect on the system of knowledge (نظام المعرفة) and the nature of decisions and actions. Note that he separates 'knowledge system' from 'decision and behavior'.
2
Distinguish Between Producing Knowledge and Influencing Decisions
The author explicitly states: 'the sexual instinct does not produce knowledge, but it influences the nature of decision and behavior.' This is the key analytical distinction. The instinct does not create new knowledge (it is not an epistemological source) but it biases or steers choices and actions.
This distinction is crucial: many mistakenly think strong emotions or instincts can themselves be sources of knowledge (e.g., 'gut feeling').
3
Analyze the Role of Sexual Repression
The author warns that sexual repression is a gateway for Satan to enter the cognitive system, leading to wrong decisions. Here, 'cognitive system' refers to the system of knowledge and decision-making. Repression distorts rational judgment.
This connects to Islamic teachings on managing desires through lawful means (halal) rather than repression.
4
Evaluate the Real-World Example
Read the anecdote about the spinster who killed her family. Ask: How does this case illustrate the author's claim? The woman's decision was driven by the sexual instinct (desire for marriage freedom) overriding rational deliberation. The author presents this as a typical example of instinct influencing behavior.
Consider how many daily decisions, from small to large, may be influenced by unrecognized instinctual drives.

Indeed, the sexual instinct has a profound effect on the system of knowledge and the nature of human decisions and actions in many cases, and it has clear effects in our daily life, positively as in the states of happiness and joy that a person feels when engaging in sexual activity, or negatively when engaging in sexual activity outside proper controls. Here we must establish an important fact: the sexual instinct does not produce knowledge, but it influences the nature of decision and behavior due to its overwhelming power. Sexual repression is also one of the entrances of Satan through which he enters into one's cognitive system and misleads him, so the heart or the soul makes a wrong decision under the influence of the sexual instinct. In this context, the Egyptian writer Salamah Musa narrates the following incident: In the year 1937, an old spinster girl killed her four brothers, two sisters, and her mother because they tightened control over her and because they repeatedly refused to marry her to her suitors under the pretext of their lack of suitability for her. She saw no alternative but to make her family the victim of her hopes in order to be free in her affair. Hundreds of similar cases occur daily in various parts of the world, where a husband or a lover is killed, all because of a decision made by the soul under the influence of the sexual instinct.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the role of the sexual instinct in human cognition and decision-making. The author begins by asserting the profound impact of the sexual instinct on the system of knowledge (نظام المعرفة) and the nature of human decisions and actions. He notes both positive effects (happiness during proper sexual activity) and negative effects (when outside proper controls). He then establishes a crucial fact: the sexual instinct does not create knowledge (لا تصنع المعرفة) but it does influence decision and behavior due to its overwhelming power. The author further warns that sexual repression is a gateway for Satan to enter the cognitive system, leading to wrong decisions. To illustrate, he recounts a story from Salamah Musa about a woman who killed her family to gain freedom from restrictions on marriage, as a real-world example of how the instinct can drive extreme actions. The passage thus sets the stage for presenting Islamic solutions to manage this instinct, though those solutions are introduced later. The author's method is to first identify the problem—the instinct's influence—and then later offer corrective measures from the Quran and Sunnah.
