The Empty Mind and Knowledge Acquisition
Author states tabula rasa, knowledge through experience, citing Imam Ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the tabula rasa position in Islamic epistemology and distinguish it from the concept of fitra.

Core Idea
The mind is born empty; knowledge unfolds through experience.

How do we come to know anything? Are we born with innate ideas or is the mind a blank slate? This passage addresses these fundamental questions in Islamic epistemology. The author takes a clear stance: humans enter the world with an empty mind, and all knowledge, even self-evident truths, develops over time through interaction with the external world. The saying of Imam Ali (peace be upon him) is used to support this empirical view of knowledge acquisition.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the author's claim about the mind at birth
Read the first sentence: the author says man comes to this world 'empty-minded' (khali al-dhihn) of any knowledge, including self-evident knowledge (al-ma'arif al-badihiyya). What is being denied? The innateness of any knowledge.
2
2. Locate the concept of potential knowledge
The passage states that knowledge is 'potentially latent' (mukhtamara bil-quwwa) in the soul. This means the capacity for knowledge is present, but not its actuality. How does potential become actual? Through contact with external realities via the tools of knowledge (senses, intellect, etc.).
3
3. Analyze the supporting authority (Imam Ali)
The author quotes Imam Ali: 'The intellect is an innate disposition that increases with knowledge and experience.' This distinguishes the intellect as a potential faculty (ghariza) from actual knowledge. The intellect grows, it is not a repository of pre-existing knowledge.
Note: This is a shift from the typical Mantiq view that badihiyyat are necessary and innate.
4
4. Contrast with the concept of fitra
In Islamic thought, fitra often implies an innate disposition to recognize God. How does this passage's tabula rasa relate? The author does not mention fitra directly, but the quote about 'ghariza' (innate disposition) can be seen as a kind of fitra. However, the emphasis is on the emptiness of specific knowledge, not the absence of a natural capacity. Reflect on how this might affect the epistemological foundation for proving God's existence.

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological question of how humans acquire knowledge. The author advocates a tabula rasa position: the mind at birth is devoid of any knowledge, including self-evident truths. However, knowledge exists potentially (bil-quwwa) in the soul. Through interaction with the external world via the senses and other means of knowledge, these potentialities become actualized. The author supports this with a saying of Imam Ali that the intellect is an innate disposition that grows with learning and experience. This emphasizes the empirical aspect of knowledge acquisition.

Distinguishing Conception (Tasawwur) and Assent (Tasdiq)
Author divides human perception into conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a mere concept (tasawwur) and a judgment of truth (tasdiq) in examples from Islamic sciences such as fiqh and kalam.

Core Idea
Perception is not just having an image—it requires a judgment about reality.

In Islamic logic, all knowledge begins with perception. But not every perception is a full-fledged knowledge. The author here introduces the foundational division between simply conceiving something and actually assenting to its truth. Understanding this split is crucial because all arguments in kalam, fiqh, and usul rest on either conceptual definitions or truth-claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Read the first sentence: human perception is divided into two main divisions. What are they called? Write down the Arabic terms.
These are التصور and التصديق.
2
Analyze tasawwur definition
Find the definition of tasawwur: 'existence of an image of a meaning in our perceptive faculties'. Notice there is no judgment—just a mental picture. The door-knocking example illustrates this.
Conception is passive receipt of an image.
3
Analyze tasdiq definition
Tasdiq is defined as 'the judgment of the soul about the existence of a reality behind the conception'. It adds a truth claim. In the example, knowing the person's name is a judgment.
Assent involves affirming or denying correspondence with reality.
4
Apply to the provided example
In the door-knocking scenario: the conception (tasawwur) is that someone is at the door; the assent (tasdiq) is that it is specifically Muhammad. Identify which part is which.
This shows how both operations work together in actual perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان الادراك البشري ينقسم على قسمين رئيسين وهما التصور والتصديق فالتصور عبارة عن وجود صورة لمعنى من المعاني في مداركنا الخاصة. فعندما يدق أحد ما عليك باب الدار فانك ستتصور وجود شخص ما وراء الباب أما التصديق وهو القسم الآخر من الادراك فهو عبارة عن حكم النفس بوجود حقيقة من الحقائق وراء التصور كمعرفتك بأسم الشخص الذي دق باب الدار هو محمد. ومادامت النفس في البدن فانها تتسلم المعرفة بجميع الطرق المعرفة الحسية) ( إستنتاجات العقل وبصائره ) ( رغبات الفطرة ) ( إشارات الايحاء ) ( التسديد الالهي بالتعليم ) ( أضواء الهداية ) ( الرؤيا المنامية ) وتختمر هذه الطرق في مرحلتي التصور والتصديق ومن ثم تتخذ النفس قرارها المناس.
Human perception is divided into two main divisions: conception and assent. Conception is the existence of an image of a meaning among meanings in our particular perceptive faculties. So when someone knocks on the door of the house, you will conceive the existence of a person behind the door. As for assent, which is the other division of perception, it is the judgment of the soul about the existence of a reality behind the conception, like your knowing that the name of the person who knocked on the door is Muhammad. And as long as the soul is in the body, it receives knowledge through all the methods of knowledge: sensory perceptions, conclusions of the intellect and its insights, desires of the innate disposition, signals of inspiration, divine guidance through teaching, lights of guidance, and dreams. And these methods are processed in the two stages of conception and assent, and then the soul takes its appropriate decision.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the two primary divisions of human perception: tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by stating that human perception is divided into two main parts. Tasawwur is described as having a mental image or concept of something in our faculties of perception; for example, when someone knocks on a door, you conceive that there is a person behind it. This is a simple representation without any judgment of truth or falsity. Tasdiq, on the other hand, involves the soul's judgment about the reality of something beyond the mere conception, such as knowing that the person who knocked is Muhammad. This judgment affirms that the concept corresponds to reality. The passage then lists various methods through which the soul acquires knowledge while in the body: sensory perception, intellectual deductions, innate inclinations, inspiration, divine teaching, guidance, and dreams. These methods are then processed through the two stages of conception and assent, leading to the soul making a decision. The passage highlights that perception is not merely passive reception but involves both conceptualization and judgment, and that multiple epistemological sources contribute to this process.

Key Terms

التصديق
الإدراك
النفس

Key Takeaway
The foundation of all knowledge in Islamic logic is the division between bare conception (tasawwur) and judgment of truth (tasdiq).

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
10 min
Distinguishing Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author defines tasawwur and tasdiq as the two divisions of human perception with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) by analyzing their definitions and roles in the knowledge acquisition process as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Tasawwur is apprehension without judgment; tasdiq adds a truth claim.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of human perception into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The passage defines each with concrete examples and situates them within the soul's broader knowledge acquisition process. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how Islamic thinkers understand cognition and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
Find the terms tasawwur and tasdiq in the Arabic text. Note that they are introduced as the two main parts of human perception (al-idrak).
2
Extract the definition of tasawwur
Tasawwur is defined as 'the presence of an image of a meaning in our individual perceptions' without any judgment. The example of a door knock illustrates: you conceive a person behind the door but do not yet judge who it is.
Tasawwur is a simple cognitive representation.
3
Extract the definition of tasdiq
Tasdiq is defined as 'the soul's judgment of the existence of a reality behind the conception.' The example shows: you know the person's name (Muhammad) — that is a truth claim involving belief.
Tasdiq involves a truth value (truth or falsehood).
4
Compare the two stages
Tasawwur is a necessary preliminary to tasdiq; tasdiq adds a judgment. The passage lists various knowledge sources (senses, intellect, etc.) that are processed in both stages to produce decisions.
Without tasawwur, there can be no object for judgment.
5
Summarize the soul's role
The soul is described as the independent fortress and vessel of knowledge. It receives inputs from multiple channels and processes them through tasawwur and tasdiq before making decisions.
The soul is the cognitive agent, not the brain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان الادراك البشري ينقسم على قسمين رئيسين وهما التصور والتصديق فالتصور عبارة عن وجود صورة لمعنى من المعاني في مداركنا الخاصة. فعندما يدق أحد ما عليك باب الدار فانك ستتصور وجود شخص ما وراء الباب أما التصديق وهو القسم الآخر من الادراك فهو عبارة عن حكم النفس بوجود حقيقة من الحقائق وراء التصور كمعرفتك بأسم الشخص الذي دق باب الدار هو محمد. ومادامت النفس في البدن فانها تتسلم المعرفة بجميع الطرق المعرفة الحسية ( إستنتاجات العقل وبصائره ) ( رغبات الفطرة ) ( إشارات الايحاء ) (التسديد الالهي بالتعليم ) ( أضواء الهداية ) ( الرؤيا المنامية ) وتختمر هذه الطرق في مرحلتي التصور والتصديق ومن ثم تتخذ النفس قرارها المناس.

الخلاصة : النفس شخصية مستقلة وهي حصن المعرفة الاصيل ووعاؤها الكبير ومحركها الاول هي السيد الاكبر في أعماق الذات لها سلطان على المادة ، البدن الجسد وهي نافذة على الغيب وما وراء الطبيعة فالمعارف تأتي الى النفس من كل حدب وصوب والقوى تتجاذبها ليلا ونهارا.
Human perception is divided into two main parts: conception and assent. Conception is the presence of an image of a meaning in our individual perceptions. For example, when someone knocks on your door, you conceive a person behind the door. As for assent, which is the other part of perception, it is the soul's judgment of the existence of a reality behind the conception, such as your knowing that the name of the person who knocked on the door is Muhammad. As long as the soul is in the body, it receives knowledge through all the ways of knowledge: sensory knowledge, the mind's deductions and insights, innate desires, signals of inspiration, divine assistance through teaching, lights of guidance, and dreams. These ways are processed in the two stages of conception and assent, and then the soul makes its appropriate decision. Summary: The soul is an independent entity; it is the original fortress of knowledge, its great vessel, and its first mover. It is the greatest master in the depths of the self; it has authority over matter, the body; and it is a window to the unseen and the metaphysical. Knowledge comes to the soul from every direction, and the forces pull it night and day.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental divisions of human perception (al-idrak) as tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The author begins by stating that human perception is divided into two main parts. He defines tasawwur as the presence of a mental image of a meaning in our perceptions, giving a concrete example: when someone knocks at the door, you conceive a person behind the door without any judgment. Tasdiq is then defined as the soul's judgment that a reality exists behind the conception, such as knowing the identity of the person as Muhammad. This distinction is standard in Islamic logic: tasawwur is apprehension without judgment, while tasdiq involves a truth claim. The text then lists the various channels of knowledge the soul receives while in the body: senses, intellect, innate desires, inspiration, divine teaching, guidance, and dreams. These are processed in the two stages, leading to the soul's decision. The summary paragraph affirms the soul's independent nature as the primary repository of knowledge, with authority over the body and access to the unseen. All Islamic examples used must originate from Islamic sciences; here the example of a person at the door is generic but the concepts are foundational for fiqh and kalam, e.g., tasawwur of 'salat' and tasdiq of its obligation.

Memory and Recollection: The Epistemological Distinction
Author explains memory as storage and distinguishes it from recollection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between memory (dhakira) as passive storage and recollection (tadhakkur) as active awareness using examples from Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Memory stores; recollection retrieves with awareness.

This passage clarifies a crucial epistemological distinction: memory (al-dhakira) is the faculty that stores sensory images, like a dictionary and instant translator, while recollection (al-tadhakkur) is the conscious act of calling them forth. Understanding this difference is essential for analysing how knowledge is preserved and accessed, particularly in Islamic sciences such as hadith preservation and Quranic exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two faculties
Find where the author defines memory (al-dhakira) and recollection (al-tadhakkur). Notice that memory is described as deposit and storage, while recollection is described as awareness (al-wa'y) and focused cognition.
The author uses everyday examples (returning home, finding items in a shop) to illustrate the depositing of images in memory.
2
Understand the mechanism of memory
The author explains that memory operates through preservation (al-hifz), which fixes the known image so it cannot change or disappear. Note the biological point about nerve cells not dividing to preserve experiences.
Memory is likened to a dictionary and instant translator, indicating its vast capacity and quick retrieval.
3
Understand the nature of recollection
Recollection is defined as awareness (al-wa'y), which is the conscious preservation of something in the soul. The author quotes Quranic verses about 'the hearing ear' (al-wā'iya) and poetic examples to show that recollection is an active cognitive act.
Contrast the passive storage of memory with the active, focused awareness of recollection.
4
Apply the distinction
Using an example from Islamic sciences: A hafiz (memorizer of the Quran) has the Quran stored in his memory through hifz. When he recites a verse consciously, that is tadhakkur—the act of bringing the stored knowledge into awareness.
This distinction helps in understanding the difference between mere retention (hifz) and conscious recall (tadhakkur).

كيف ترجع الى بيتك ؟ انك تعرف مكانه وكيف عرفت مكانه ؟ ان مكانه قد اودع في ذاكرتك . وانت في محلك التجاري كيف تأخذ من هذا المكان هذه القطعة ومن هذا المكان هذه القطعة . لان هذه القطع كلها مودعة في ذاكرتك ، ان الانسان اذا عاش ستين عاما فهناك من الصور التي تختزنها ذاكرته مايزيد عن مليارات المعلومات فلو اردنا ان ننسخ هذه المعلومات في كتب لاحتاجت الى آلاف المجلدات. وثمة ذاكرة للمسموعات وذاكرة للمشمومات وذاكرة للمبصرات وذاكرة للالوان وذاكرة للاسماء فاذا قدم اليك عطر شممته تقول اسمه كذا وهكذا في الذوقيات ويقول بعض العلماء ان الذاكرة قاموس ومترجم فوري ويتجسد عملها من خلال الحفظ (وهو) ضبط الصورة المعلومة بحيث لا يتطرق إليها التغيير و الزوال )) ( ان الخلية العصبية لا تنقسم ولاتموت فلو انها انقسمت وماتت لفقد الانسان خبراته كلها فيقول لك فلان ان خبرتي في الطب اربعون عاما وانا خبرتي في القانون خمسون عاما ولو فقد ذاكرته لفقد كل خبراته دفعة واحدة فما هي الذاكرة وما هو التذكر ؟ التذكر وعي وهو الادراك المركز { الوعي حفظ الحديث ونحوه يقال وعيته في نفسي. قال تعالى ( لنجعلها لكم تذكرة وتعيها اذن واعية والايعاء حفظ الامتعة في الوعاء قال تعالى (وجمع فاوعى قال الشاعر والشر أخبث ما أوعيت من زاد . ولا تماسك للنفس من دون وعيه
How do you return to your house? You know its place, and how did you know its place? Its place has been deposited in your memory. When you are in your shop, how do you take this piece from this place and that piece from that place? Because all these pieces are deposited in your memory. If a human lives sixty years, there are images stored in his memory amounting to billions of pieces of information. If we wanted to copy this information into books, it would require thousands of volumes. There is a memory for sounds, a memory for smells, a memory for sights, a memory for colors, and a memory for names. So if a perfume you smelled is presented to you, you say its name is such-and-such, and likewise for tastes. Some scholars say that memory is a dictionary and an instant translator. Its work is embodied in preservation (hifz), which is the fixation of the known image such that neither change nor disappearance can reach it. The nerve cell does not divide and does not die. If it were to divide and die, a person would lose all his experiences. So someone will tell you: my experience in medicine is forty years, and my experience in law is fifty years. If he lost his memory, he would lose all his experiences at once. So what is memory and what is recollection? Recollection is awareness, and it is focused cognition. Awareness is the preservation of a hadith or similar; one says: 'I have preserved it (wa'aytuhu) in my soul.' He said, exalted: 'That We may make it a reminder for you, and that the hearing ear (wa'iyat) may heed.' And al-i'a' is the storage of luggage in a container. He said, exalted: 'And gathered [wealth] and hoarded (fa-aw'a).' The poet said: 'And evil is the worst thing you have stored (aw'ayt) as provision.' There is no cohesion for the soul without its awareness.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of memory (al-dhakira) and its function. The author begins by using everyday examples: how we know the location of our house or items in a shop, attributing this to images deposited in memory. He emphasizes the vast capacity of memory, storing billions of pieces of information over a lifetime. The author then lists different types of memory: for sounds, smells, sights, colors, and names, illustrating how we can identify a familiar perfume by its smell. He cites scholars who describe memory as a dictionary and instant translator, operating through preservation (hifz), which fixes a known image so it cannot change or disappear. He supports this by noting that nerve cells do not divide or die; otherwise, we would lose all experience. Finally, he distinguishes between memory (dhakira) and recollection (tadhakkur): recollection is conscious, focused cognition. He explains that awareness (wa'y) is the preservation of hadith, quoting Quranic verses and poetry to illustrate the concept of preservation and storage. Thus, the passage defines memory as the faculty that stores sensory data and distinguishes it from the active process of recollection.

Key Terms
الذاكرة
التذكر
الحفظ
الوعي

C) To produce new knowledge

Key Takeaway
Memory stores sensory images through preservation; recollection is the conscious, focused awareness that retrieves them.

The Quranic Theory: External and Internal Sources of Knowledge
Author lists Quranic truths and argues prophets/scriptures are primary knowledge sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two sources of knowledge in the Quranic theory (external prophets/scriptures and internal soul insights) and cite Quranic examples of empirical truths.

Core Idea
The Quran presents both external revelation and innate reason as complementary sources of knowledge.

In this passage, the author lays out the Quranic theory of knowledge, which affirms that truth is found in both divine revelation and human reason. He begins by listing empirical facts mentioned in the Quran, then explains how prophets unlock the meaning of scripture. Finally, he cites jurists who compare scriptures to an 'external prophet' and innate insights to an 'internal prophet'. This lesson will help you identify these two sources and see how they work together.

Guided Path
1
List the Quranic truths cited
The author gives three examples of empirical truths from the Quran. Identify each one and note the verse referenced.
2
Understand the need for prophetic interpretation
The author says scriptures need explanation (tabyin) and prophets fulfill that role. Consider why the Quran alone might not be sufficient without a living teacher.
3
Distinguish external and internal sources
The jurists describe scriptures and messengers as an 'external prophet' and the soul's insights and axiomatic truths as an 'internal prophet'. Identify what each refers to.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that prophets and scriptures are two springs (yanbu'an) of knowledge. Reflect on how the internal prophet (reason) and external prophet (revelation) complement each other in the Quranic worldview.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة
حقيقة : ان الجبال تحفظ للارض توازنها في مواجهة الماء (وَأَلْقَى فِي الْأَرْضِ رَوَاسِيَ أَنْ تَمِيدَ بِكُمْ)
حقيقة : إن المياه العذبة والمياه المالحة لا تمتزجان عند التلاقي (وَهُوَ الَّذِي مَرَجَ الْبَحرينِ هَذَا عَذَبٌ فُرَات وَهَذَا مِلْحٌ أَجَاج وجعل بينهما برزخًا وحجرًا محجوراً)
حقيقة : أن الارض متحركة وترى الجبال تحسبها جامدة وهي تمر مر السحاب والجبال ملتصقة بالارض.
إن الكتب السماوية في حاجة الى بيان معانيها وتوضيحها وكشف المراد منها وكان الانبياء يقومون بهذا الدور ويفيضون على الناس بمزيد من المعلومات في مختلف نواحي الحياة فشكلوا مصدرا للمعرفة كما تقول نظرية القرآن . أن الفقهاء والمفسرين يقولون ان الكتب السماوية والرسل هم بمثابة نبي خارجي وان بصائر النفس وثوابتها البديهية هما بمثابة نبي داخلي والخلاصة : ان الانبياء والكتب السماوية ينبوعان من ينابيع المعرفة كما تقول نظرية القرآن الكريم .
Theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. Truth: that the mountains preserve the earth's balance in facing water (And He cast into the earth firm mountains lest it shake with you). Truth: that fresh water and salty water do not mix when they meet (And He is the One who released the two seas, this fresh sweet and this salty bitter, and made between them a barrier and a forbidding partition). Truth: that the earth is moving and you see the mountains thinking they are stationary while they pass like the passing of clouds, and the mountains are attached to the earth. Indeed, the heavenly scriptures are in need of explanation of their meanings and clarification and uncovering of what is intended from them, and the prophets used to carry out this role and bestow upon people additional information in various aspects of life, thus they formed a source of knowledge as the Quranic theory says. The jurists and exegetes say that the heavenly scriptures and the messengers are like an external prophet, and the insights of the soul and its axiomatic constants are like an internal prophet. The conclusion: that the prophets and heavenly scriptures are two springs from the springs of knowledge as the Quranic theory says.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion on the Quranic theory of knowledge (nazariyyat al-ma'rifa). The author begins by citing three empirical truths mentioned in the Quran as examples of the knowledge contained in scripture: mountains stabilizing the earth, the barrier between fresh and salt water, and the motion of the earth. Then he argues that heavenly scriptures require interpretation, which was performed by the prophets. The prophets thereby become a source of knowledge, providing additional information to people. The author then cites the view of jurists and exegetes that scriptures and messengers constitute an 'external prophet', while the soul's innate insights and axiomatic truths constitute an 'internal prophet'. The conclusion is that prophets and scriptures are two fundamental sources (yanbu'an) of knowledge according to the Quranic theory. Note that this passage does not present a formal logical argument but rather an epistemological exposition rooted in Islamic revelation.

Key Terms
نظرية المعرفة (nazariyyat al-ma'rifa): The Quranic theory of knowledge, encompassing revelation and innate insights as sources. [Note: Stable in modern Islamic philosophy; classical mantiq uses 'ilm instead.]
الكتب السماوية (al-kutub al-samawiyya): Heavenly scriptures, specifically the Quran and earlier divine books, as sources of knowledge.
بصائر النفس (basa'ir al-nafs): Innate insights of the soul, distinguished from external scripture. [Note: In classical texts, usually refer to intellectual intuitions; here contrasted with 'external prophet'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Quranic theory of knowledge recognizes both external (prophets and scriptures) and internal (soul's innate insights) sources of knowledge, working together.

Piety as the Path to Extraordinary Knowledge and Certainty
Author explains how piety leads to intuitive knowledge and certainty, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how piety (taqwa) leads to extraordinary intuitive knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-shuhudiyya) and certainty (al-yaqin) according to the Quranic verses cited, and distinguish between the roles of discursive reasoning and direct witnessing in this process.

Core Idea
Piety unlocks a special light (furqan) that grants certainty beyond mere reasoning.

In this passage, the author argues that the Quran promises extraordinary knowledge and certainty to the pious. This is not ordinary knowledge; it is a direct, intuitive witnessing (shuhud) that culminates in unshakable certainty (yaqin). The author uses Quranic verses to show that piety is the key to receiving divine guidance and even miraculous powers. Understanding this helps us see the epistemological foundation of Islamic spirituality—that true certainty integrates both rational proof and direct divine illumination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The passage claims that piety (taqwa) leads to extraordinary intuitive knowledge and certainty. Locate where the author states this explicitly in the Arabic.
The claim is that the pious person ascends in knowledge until witnessing the unseen.
2
Extract the Quranic evidence
List the four verses cited: (1) al-Anfal 29 on furqan, (2) Yusuf 24 on turning away evil, (3) al-Hadid 28 on double mercy and light, (4) al-Baqarah 282 linking piety to divine teaching.
3
Analyze the concept of furqan
Furqan is described as a special light in the heart. Note that it functions in two ways: through reasoning (burhan) and through unveiling (mukashafa). This dual method is key to the author's epistemology.
The term furqan here is not the Quran itself, but an inner criterion.
4
Understand the relationship between worship and yaqin
The verse 'worship until certainty' shows that worship is a means, not the end. Yaqin is defined by Tabatabai as 'intensification of mental perception that does not accept removal and weakness'.
Yaqin is the ultimate goal, not mere worship or even knowledge as an endpoint.
5
Connect to your own intellectual training
This teaches that for the pious, knowledge can come both through rational proof (like logical arguments in kalam) and through direct spiritual insight (like the 'ayn al-yaqin of the Sufis). The same truth can be known in two ways.
This duality is important for understanding how Islamic epistemology integrates reason and revelation.

المعرفة الشهودية الخارقة:
إن في القرآن الكريم آيات كثيرة تصرح بان الأنسان المتقي المتحلي بالفضائل المتنزه عن الرذائل ترعاه عناية الله تعالى وتفيض عليه الهداية بعد الهداية والعلم بعد العلم ولايزال يرقى مدارج المعرفة حتى يبلغ مقام شهود الغيب ويعطى قدرات خارقة تكريما من الله سبحانه لعباده المخلصين وفيما يأتي بعض هذه الآيات قال تعالى (يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ تَتَّقُوا اللَّهَ يَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ فُرْقَانًا أي يجعل لكم في قلوبكم نورا خاصا تفرقون به بين الصالح والطالح تارة بالبرهنة والاستدلال واخرى بالشهود والمكاشفة وقد تصل العناية الالهية لمن اخلص لله الى مراحل متقدمة تتمثل في صرف السوء والفحشاء عنه فهذا رجل ( رأى بُرْهَانَ رَبِّهِ كَذَلِكَ لِنَصْرِفَ عَنْهُ السُّوءَ وَالْفَحْشَاءَ إِنَّهُ مِنْ عِبَادِنَا الْمُخْلَصين) وقال تعالى (يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا اتَّقُوا اللَّهَ وَآمِنُوا بِرَسُولِهِ يُؤْتِكُم كفْلَينِ مِنْ رَحمتِهِ وَيَجْعَلْ لَكُمْ نُورًا تَمْشُونَ بِهِ) (وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ وَيُعَلِّمُكُمُ اللهُ) ونلاحظ هنا الصلة الواضحة التي تربط بين التقى والتعليم لاحظ الآية (واعبد رَبِّكَ حَتَّى يَأْتِيَكَ الْيَقينُ) فالعبادة هدف أوسط أما الهدف الاسمى فهو اليقين ( اليقين شيء لا يتصور غيره بحال) وبعبارة العلامة الطبطبائي ( اليقين: هو اشتداد الإدراك الذهني بحيث لا يقبل الزوال والوهن وهناك ثمار متعددة لمراتب اليقين في المنظور القرآني (كلا لو تعلمون علم اليقين. لترون الجحيم. ثم لترونها عين اليقين ان الرؤية أو الاطلاع على الجحيم أو رؤية الجنة من ثمرات علم اليقين بالمعارف الالهية وان المشاهدة
Extraordinary intuitive knowledge: Indeed, in the Holy Quran there are many verses that declare that the pious human, adorned with virtues, free from vices, is cared for by the care of Allah, and guidance after guidance and knowledge after knowledge are bestowed upon him, and he continues to ascend the ranks of knowledge until he reaches the station of witnessing the unseen and is given extraordinary powers as an honor from Allah for His devoted servants. Below are some of these verses: He, exalted, said: 'O you who have believed, if you fear Allah, He will grant you a criterion' - meaning He will place in your hearts a special light by which you distinguish between the righteous and the corrupt, sometimes by proof and reasoning, and other times by witnessing and unveiling. And divine care may reach, for those who are sincere to Allah, advanced stages represented in averting evil and immorality from them. So this is a man (he saw the proof of his Lord; thus We turned away from him evil and immorality; indeed, he was of Our devoted servants). And He said: 'O you who have believed, fear Allah and believe in His Messenger; He will give you a double portion of His mercy and make for you a light by which you walk.' And 'Fear Allah, and Allah will teach you.' And we note here the clear connection that links piety and education. Notice the verse: 'And worship your Lord until there comes to you certainty (yaqin).' So worship is an intermediate goal, while the ultimate goal is certainty (yaqin is something that cannot be conceived otherwise). In the words of Allamah Tabatabai: 'Yaqin is the intensification of mental perception such that it does not accept removal and weakness.' And there are multiple fruits of the levels of certainty from the Quranic perspective: 'No! If you knew with the knowledge of certainty, you would surely see Hellfire. Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty.' Indeed, seeing or witnessing Hellfire or seeing Paradise are among the fruits of the knowledge of certainty in divine knowledge, and that witnessing...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the concept of extraordinary intuitive knowledge (al-ma'rifa al-shuhudiyya al-khariqa). The author explains that the Quran promises that the pious person, adorned with virtues, receives divine care and is granted successive guidance and knowledge, ascending until he attains the station of witnessing the unseen (shuhud al-ghayb) and is given extraordinary powers. The author cites several Quranic verses: the first (Ayat 29 of Surah al-Anfal) speaks of God granting a 'furqan' (criterion) – a special light to distinguish right from wrong, both through demonstration (burhan) and through direct witnessing (shuhud) and unveiling (mukashafa). The second (Surah Yusuf 24) mentions how divine care turns away evil from the sincere servant. The third (Surah al-Hadid 28) promises a double mercy and a light. The fourth (Surah al-Baqarah 282) links piety with being taught by Allah. The author then highlights the clear connection between taqwa and knowledge. Finally, he cites the verse commanding worship until certainty (yaqin) arrives, explaining that worship is a means but yaqin is the ultimate aim. He quotes Allamah Tabatabai's definition of yaqin as the intensification of mental perception that is unshakable. The passage concludes by mentioning the fruits of yaqin: the vision of Hellfire or Paradise as a result of knowledge of certainty.

The Three Levels of Certainty
Author defines the three levels of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three levels of certainty (al-yaqin) — 'ilm al-yaqin, 'ayn al-yaqin, and haqq al-yaqin — and identify their Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Certainty is not monolithic: it ascends from rational proof to direct vision.

In this passage, the author begins by defining the three levels of certainty, grounding them in Quranic verses. This classification is essential for understanding the epistemology of Islamic mysticism and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three levels
Find the Arabic names for each level as the author presents them. Note the order: the author starts with 'Ayn al-Yaqin then mentions a higher stage, Haqq al-Yaqin.
2
Locate the Quranic citations
For each level, the author cites a Quranic verse: 'Ayn al-Yaqin from Quran 102:7, Haqq al-Yaqin from Quran 56:95.
The passage does not explicitly mention 'Ilm al-Yaqin, but it is implied as the first level.
3
Interpret the author's explanation
The author interprets these verses as referring to seeing Hell in this life, not just the afterlife. This interpretation elevates 'Ayn al-Yaqin and Haqq al-Yaqin to experiential realities.
4
Compare with classical Mantiq
In classical Mantiq, yaqin is often certain knowledge from demonstration. Here, the author expands it to include spiritual vision, shifting from rational to experiential certainty.

الأعلى هي ( عين اليقين ) (ثُمَّ لَتَرُونَهَا عَينَ الْيَقِينِ ) ثم مرحلة أعلى تسمى (حق اليقين قال تعالى إِنْ هَذَا لَهُوَ حَق الْيَقِينِ) والمراد رؤية الجحيم قبل يوم القيامة في هذه الحياة الدنيا لأن رؤية الجحيم يوم القيامة أمر ميسور ومتحقق لجميع المؤمنين وجميع الكافرين العالمين منهم والجاهلين. إن هذه الآيات وكثير غيرها تقول إن نافذة الفتوحات الباطنية والمكاشفات والمشاهدات تنزل على الأمثل فالأمثل من أفراد الأمة على قدر استحقاقهم وتحملهم. ورد في الحديث الشريف ( ومن زهد فيها - في الدنيا فقصر فيها أمله أعطاه الله علما بغير تعلم وهدى بغير هداية وأذهب عنه العماء وجعله بصيرا) وفي الحديث القدسي قول الله عز وجل عن العابد المجاهد (فإذا عشقني وعشقته رفعت الحجب فيما بيني وبينه) ( لايزال العبد يتقرب إلي بالنوافل حتى أحبه فإذا أحببته كنت له سمعا وبصرا وقلبا ويدا ورجلا إن دعاني أجبته وإن ناداني لبيته) . وقال عز من قائل ( عبدي أطعني حتى أجعلك مثلي أقول للشيء كن فيكون تقول للشيء كن فيكون. وفي قراءة موحدة لنصوص هذا الفصل نرى الصور الآتية : الذين آمنوا بالله ورسوله) (واتقوا الله) (وعبدوا الله مخلصين) وكسبوا العلم الرصين العميق) ( وزهدوا في الدنيا) (وعشقوا الله بكل جوارحهم سيصل كل منهم الى النتائج الآتية ( يجعل له فرقانا ) ( يؤته كفلين من رحمته) ( يجعل له نورا يمشي به (يعلمه الله ( يأتيه اليقين) يرى الجحيم في الحياة الدنيا قبل الآخرة) ( يصل الى عين اليقين ) ( يعطيه الله علما بغير تعلم وهدى بغير هداية يذهب عنه العمى ويفتح بصيرته) (ترفع الحجب فيما بينه وبين (الله ويحدث الكشف . والمقصود بالكشف رفع الحجاب من الحقائق الغيبية والأمور الخفية سواء عن طريق المشاهدة كما تشاهد الصور في المرآة أو عن طريق السماع يسمع صوت الملك ولا يرى صورته أو قذف مباشر في القلب أو عن طريق الملامسة بالاتصال بين النورين أو رؤية الحقيقة خلال المنام قال علي السلام عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه المنتجبين ( كشف الله له عن السموات والأرض حتى العرش وأسفل الأرضين وعن الإمام علي عليه السلام قال بعد أن رأى كل الحقائق قال لو كشف لي الغطاء ما ازددت يقينا) وقيل إن الكشف يعني الحصول على تجليات الاسم العليم الحكيم بشكل مجرد عن المادة وآثارها )
The highest is 'Ayn al-Yaqin' (Then you will surely see it with the eye of certainty). Then a higher stage called 'Haqq al-Yaqin' (Indeed this is the truth of certainty). And the meaning is seeing Hell before the Day of Resurrection in this worldly life, because seeing Hell on the Day of Resurrection is easy and realized for all believers and all disbelievers, both the learned and the ignorant. Indeed these verses and many others say that the window of inner openings, unveilings, and witnesses descends upon the best then the best of the individuals of the Ummah according to their merit and capacity. It is reported in the noble hadith: 'Whoever abstains from it (the world), and shortens his hope in it, Allah gives him knowledge without learning, guidance without guidance, removes blindness from him, and makes him seeing.' And in the sacred hadith, the saying of Allah Almighty about the worshipping mujahid: 'When he loves Me and I love him, I lift the veils between me and him.' 'The servant continues to draw near to Me with supererogatory acts until I love him, and when I love him, I become his hearing, his sight, his heart, his hand, and his foot; if he calls Me, I answer him, if he asks Me, I grant him.' And He said, Glory be to Him: 'O my servant, obey Me until I make you like Me; I say to a thing 'Be' and it is, and you say to a thing 'Be' and it is.' And from a unified reading of the texts of this chapter, we see the following forms: those who believed in Allah and His Messenger, feared Allah, worshipped Allah sincerely, acquired solid deep knowledge, abstained from the world, and loved Allah with all their faculties — each of them will reach the following outcomes: He will make for him a criterion, He will give him a double portion of His mercy, He will make for him a light with which to walk, He will teach him, certainty will come to him, he will see Hell in this worldly life before the Hereafter, he will reach 'Ayn al-Yaqin, Allah will give him knowledge without learning and guidance without guidance, He will remove blindness from him and open his insight, the veils will be lifted between him and Allah, and unveiling occurs. And what is meant by unveiling is the lifting of the veil from unseen realities and hidden matters, whether through witnessing as one sees images in a mirror, or through hearing—hearing the voice of an angel without seeing his form, or a direct casting into the heart, or through touch by connection between two lights, or seeing the truth through dreams. Ali (peace be upon him) said about the Prophet (PBUH) and his chosen companions: 'Allah unveiled for him the heavens and the earth up to the Throne and beneath the earths.' And about Imam Ali (peace be upon him) it is said that after he saw all realities, he said: 'If the cover were removed for me, I would not increase in certainty.' And it is said that unveiling means obtaining the manifestations of the All-Knowing, All-Wise Name in a manner abstracted from matter and its effects.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition defining three levels of certainty ('ilm al-yaqin, 'ayn al-yaqin, haqq al-yaqin) and describing how a believer attains mystical knowledge (kashf). The author begins by specifying the highest level as 'Ayn al-Yaqin, citing Quran 102:7, then a higher stage 'Haqq al-Yaqin from Quran 56:95. He interprets these as referring to seeing Hell in this life, not just the afterlife. This is supported by hadith about the reward of zuhd (asceticism) and the sacred hadith about divine love leading to the lifting of veils. The author then summarizes the qualities and outcomes for true believers: faith, taqwa, sincerity, deep knowledge, zuhd, and divine love. The outcomes include receiving a furqan (criterion), double mercy, light, knowledge without learning, and ultimately kashf (unveiling). Kashf is defined as the removal of the veil from unseen realities, through various means like vision, hearing, inspiration, or dreams. The author supports this with the famous saying of Imam Ali that if the veil were lifted, his certainty would not increase—indicating that the highest certainty (haqq al-yaqin) is direct vision, not inferential. The passage uses Quran and hadith as epistemological sources, emphasizing that such knowledge is granted by Allah to those who purify themselves.

Key Terms
اليقين
عين اليقين
حق اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest level of certainty (yaqin)?
A) 'Ilm al-yaqin
B) 'Ayn al-yaqin
C) Haqq al-yaqin
D) Zann

Key Takeaway
Certainty in Islamic epistemology has three degrees: knowledge from proof, seeing with the eye, and direct experiential truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a Quranic verse that corresponds to each of the three levels of certainty as defined by the author.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate
8 min
The Spiritual Path to Kashf
Author cites hadith to show the path to kashf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the spiritual prerequisites for attaining kashf (unveiling) as mentioned in the hadith and sacred hadith, and describe the promised outcomes.

Core Idea
Kashf is a gift from Allah granted through asceticism and divine love.

The author supports the path to kashf with prophetic and divine traditions. These texts outline the conditions of zuhd (abstinence) and divine love, and the resulting knowledge and unveiling.

Guided Path
1
Extract the hadith on zuhd
Read the first hadith: 'Whoever abstains from it (the world) and shortens his hope...' Identify the prerequisite (zuhd) and the outcomes: knowledge without learning, guidance, removal of blindness, and becoming seeing.
2
Analyze the sacred hadith on love
In the sacred hadith, the condition is that the servant loves Allah and Allah loves him. The result is lifting of veils, and Allah becoming his senses and limbs. Note the progression: from love to unveiling.
3
Interpret the third hadith
The third hadith says: 'Obey Me until I make you like Me...' This indicates a high state where the servant's actions are effective by divine power. It shows the culmination of the path: a state of nearness where kashf is complete.

The Nature and Means of Kashf
Author summarizes the path and defines kashf and its means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the different means of kashf (unveiling) as described in the passage and explain the significance of the saying of Imam Ali.

Core Idea
Kashf is the lifting of the veil from the unseen, achieved through multiple sensory and spiritual avenues.

The author summarizes the conditions and outcomes of the spiritual journey, then defines kashf and lists its various means. He concludes with the powerful statements of the Prophet and Imam Ali, showing the highest level of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for attainment
From the summary, list the qualities: faith, taqwa, sincerity, deep knowledge, zuhd, and love of Allah. These are prerequisites for the outcomes.
2
List the outcomes
The outcomes include: criterion, double mercy, light, knowledge, certainty, seeing Hell in this life, reaching ayn al-yaqin, knowledge without learning, removal of blindness, and kashf. Note the progression.
3
Analyze the definition of kashf
The author defines kashf as lifting the veil from unseen realities. He lists five means: witnessing (like seeing in a mirror), hearing (voice of angel), direct inspiration (qadhf in the heart), touch (connection between lights), and dreams.
4
Interpret the saying of Imam Ali
Imam Ali's statement 'If the cover were removed for me, I would not increase in certainty' indicates that he already possesses haqq al-yaqin, the highest certainty. This shows that the highest level is direct, unshakeable knowledge.

And what is meant by unveiling is the lifting of the veil from unseen realities and hidden matters, whether through witnessing as one sees images in a mirror, or through hearing—hearing the voice of an angel without seeing his form, or a direct casting into the heart, or through touch by connection between two lights, or seeing the truth through dreams. Ali (peace be upon him) said about the Prophet (PBUH) and his chosen companions: 'Allah unveiled for him the heavens and the earth up to the Throne and beneath the earths.' And about Imam Ali (peace be upon him) it is said that after he saw all realities, he said: 'If the cover were removed for me, I would not increase in certainty.' And it is said that unveiling means obtaining the manifestations of the All-Knowing, All-Wise Name in a manner abstracted from matter and its effects.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition defining three levels of certainty ('ilm al-yaqin, 'ayn al-yaqin, haqq al-yaqin) and describing how a believer attains mystical knowledge (kashf). The author begins by specifying the highest level as 'Ayn al-Yaqin, citing Quran 102:7, then a higher stage 'Haqq al-Yaqin from Quran 56:95. He interprets these as referring to seeing Hell in this life, not just the afterlife. This is supported by hadith about the reward of zuhd (asceticism) and the sacred hadith about divine love leading to the lifting of veils. The author then summarizes the qualities and outcomes for true believers: faith, taqwa, sincerity, deep knowledge, zuhd, and divine love. The outcomes include receiving a furqan (criterion), double mercy, light, knowledge without learning, and ultimately kashf (unveiling). Kashf is defined as the removal of the veil from unseen realities, through various means like vision, hearing, inspiration, or dreams. The author supports this with the famous saying of Imam Ali that if the veil were lifted, his certainty would not increase—indicating that the highest certainty (haqq al-yaqin) is direct vision, not inferential. The passage uses Quran and hadith as epistemological sources, emphasizing that such knowledge is granted by Allah to those who purify themselves.

عين اليقين
حق اليقين

Key Takeaway
Kashf is a multi-faceted unveiling of the unseen, granted by Allah to those who fulfil the spiritual prerequisites.

Three Necessary Conditions for Kashf
Author enumerates three necessary conditions for a soul to receive unveiling (kashf).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three conditions for receiving from the unseen as described by the gnostics.

Core Idea
Kashf requires a soul that is undeficient, pure, and fully oriented toward the unseen.

Unveiling (kashf) is not random inspiration; the gnostics (al-‘urafā’) specify precise conditions that must hold in the soul for it to receive knowledge from the unseen. This lesson unpacks those conditions and their analogies, showing how spiritual epistemology is grounded in the soul's state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition
Find the first condition: the soul's essence must not be deficient, like the soul of a child that cannot manifest knowledge due to its incompleteness. This implies that the soul must be mature and sound in its substance.
The child example shows that deficiency blocks reception, even if the child is pure.
2
Identify the second condition
The second condition is purity (safa’) from the turbidity of physical nature and the impurities of sins. The author likens this to polishing away filth and rust from a mirror.
This moral and spiritual purification is necessary for the soul to reflect truths.
3
Identify the third condition
The third condition is complete orientation (tawajjuh) toward the unseen world and turning away from worldly matters. This is compared to aligning a mirror to face an object.
Without proper orientation, even a clean mirror will not reflect the intended object.
4
Connect the conditions to epistemology
Recognize that these conditions are necessary for kashf: a deficient, impure, or misdirected soul cannot receive true unveiling. The gnostics thus set epistemic standards rooted in spiritual discipline.
This framework distinguishes genuine kashf from mere imagination or wishful thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجعل العرفاء الشرائط اللازمة لإمكان التلقي من الغيب في أمور لا بد من تحققها في النفس وهي: ١- عدم نقصان جوهرها بأن لا تكون كنفس الصبي التي لا تتجلى لها المعلومات لنقصانها. ٢- صفاؤها عن كدورات ظلمة الطبيعة وخبائث المعاصي وهو بمنزلة الصقل عن الخبث والصدأ. ٣- توجهها الكامل إلى عالم الغيب وانصرافها إلى المطلوب بأن لا تكون غارقة في الأمور الدنيوية وهي بمنزلة محاذاة المرآة.
And the gnostics posit the necessary conditions for the possibility of receiving from the unseen in matters that must be realized in the soul, and they are: 1. The absence of deficiency in its essence, such that it is not like the soul of a child in whom information does not manifest due to its deficiency. 2. Its purity from the turbidity of the darkness of nature and the impurities of sins, which is like polishing away filth and rust. 3. Its complete orientation towards the world of the unseen and its turning away to the sought object, such that it is not immersed in worldly matters, and this is like aligning the mirror.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage enumerates three epistemological conditions for receiving knowledge from the unseen (kashf). The author states that according to the gnostics (al-‘urafā’), certain conditions must exist in the soul. First, the soul must have no essential deficiency (like that of a child) that prevents it from receiving information. Second, the soul must be pure from the turbidity of physical nature and the uncleanness of sins—this is likened to polishing a mirror. Third, the soul must be completely oriented toward the unseen world and turned away from worldly matters, like aligning a mirror to reflect an image. These conditions emphasize that kashf is not arbitrary but requires spiritual maturity and purity. The mirror analogy is used in Islamic philosophy (e.g., Suhrawardi, Mulla Sadra) to explain how the soul reflects divine realities when it is clean and properly directed.

D) The soul must be oriented toward the unseen, not worldly matters.

Key Takeaway
The three conditions for kashf are an undeficient essence, purity from sin, and complete orientation toward the unseen—mirroring the soul's preparation for divine illumination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Gnostic and the Nature of Gnosis: Insights from Islamic Authorities
Author compiles quotes from Ibn Sina, Sadr al-Muta'allihin, and others to describe the state of the gnostic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key characteristics of the gnostic (al-'arif) and the nature of ma'rifa as described by Ibn Sina, Sadr al-Muta'allihin, Imam al-Rida, and al-Qushayri, and distinguish between theoretical knowledge and experiential gnosis.

Core Idea
Gnosis is not mere theory: it is the fruit of spiritual purification and divine illumination.

This passage compiles authoritative descriptions of the gnostic from major figures in Islamic philosophy and Sufism. You will see how these thinkers converge on the idea that true knowledge of God (ma'rifa) transforms the seeker, involving disengagement from bodily attachments and a direct, illuminating encounter with the divine. This lesson helps you distinguish this experiential knowledge from ordinary theoretical learning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources
Note the five authorities cited: Ibn Sina, Sadr al-Muta'allihin, Imam Zayn al-'Abidin, Imam al-Rida, and al-Qushayri. Understand their backgrounds: philosopher, mystic-philosopher, Imam, Imam, and Sufi scholar respectively.
2
Extract the core description from each source
For each authority, summarize what they say about the gnostic's state and transformation. Ibn Sina speaks of hidden and apparent matters; Sadr al-Muta'allihin describes the spirit's disengagement and resultant light; Imam Zayn al-'Abidin's prayer depicts longing and intimacy; Imam al-Rida emphasizes divine selection and infallibility; al-Qushayri defines gnosis as knowledge plus truthfulness and purification.
3
Identify common themes
Notice recurring ideas: purification or disengagement, illumination by divine light, direct knowledge of God, and transformation of the individual. All sources agree that gnosis goes beyond intellectual understanding.
Common themes include 'tajrid' (disengagement), 'nur' (light), and 'ma'rifa' as direct knowledge.
4
Note differences in emphasis
Ibn Sina focuses on hidden stations; Sadr al-Muta'allihin identifies the light with the Active Intellect; the Imams stress prayer and divine choice; al-Qushayri systematizes gnosis as moral and cognitive refinement.
These differences reflect varying contexts: falsafa, ishraq, du'a, and Sufi manual.
5
Synthesize a unified concept of ma'rifa
Conclude that gnosis (ma'rifa) in this tradition is an experiential, transformative knowledge that results from spiritual purification and divine illumination, distinct from mere academic learning.
This synthesis helps you recognize ma'rifa in later Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن سينا ( فيقول ان للعارفين مقامات ودرجات يخصون بها وهم في حياتهم الدنيا دون غيرهم فكأنهم وهم في جلابيـب مـن ابــدانهم قـد نضوها وتجردوا عنها الى عالم القدس ولهم امور خفية فيهم وامور ظاهرة عنهم يستنكرها من ينكرها ويستكبرها من يعرفها) وعلى تعبير صدر المتألهين الشيرازي ( إن الروح الانساني إذا تجرد عن البدن خرج عن وثاقه من بيت قالبه ومواطن طبعه مهاجرا الى ربه لمشاهدة آياته الكبرى وتطهر من دون المعاصي واللذات والشهوات والوساوس العادية والتعلقات لاح له نور المعرفة والايمان وملكوته الاعلى وهذا النور إذا تأكد وتجوهر كان جوهرا قدسيا يسمى عند الحكماء في لسان الحكمة النظرية بالعقل الفعال وفي لسان الشريعة النبوية بالروح القدسي وهذا النور العقلي الشديد يتلألأ فيه أسرار ما في الأرض والسماء ويترأى منه حقائق الاشياء) وفي مناجاة العارفين المروية عن الامام علي بن الحسين (ع ) ورد ( الهي فاجعلنا من الذين ترسخت اشجار الشوق اليك في حدائق صدورهم ، واخذت لوعة محبتك بمجامع قلوبهم ، فهم الى اوكار الافكار يأوون ، وفي رياض القرب والمكاشفة يرتعون ، ومن حياض المحبة بكأس الملاطفة يكرعون وعذب في معين المعاملة شربهم ، وطاب في مجلس الانس سرهم واطمأنت بالرجوع الى رب الارباب انفسهم وقرت بالنظر الى محبوبهم اعينهم الهي ما الذ خواطر الالهام بذكرك على القلوب وما احلى المسير اليك بالاوهام في مسالك الغيوب، وما اطيب طعم حبك ، وما اعذب شرب قربك)) وفي عبارة الامام علي بن موسى الرضاء السلام ان العبد اذا اختاره الله عز وجل لامور عباده شرح صدره واودع قلبه ينابيع الحكمه، والهمه العلـم الهامـا فـلـم يـع بعد بجواب، ولا يحير فيه عن الصواب، فهو معصوم مؤيد موفق مسدد، قد امن من الخطايا والزلل والعثـار يخصه الله بذلك ليكون حجته على عباده وشاهده على خلقه، وذلك فضل الله يوتيه من يشاء والله ذو الفضل العظيم) . وبتعبير عبد الكريم إبن هوازن القشبيري ( ٣٧ - ٤٧٥ هـ ) ( كل عالم بالله تعالى عارف وكـل عـارف عالم وعند هؤلاء القوم المعرفة صفة من عرف الحق سبحانه بأسمائه وصفاته، ثم صدق الله في معاملاته، ثم تُنفى عنه أخلاقه الرديئة وآفاته ثم طال بالباب وقوفه ودام بالقلـب إعتكافه فحظي الله تعالى بجميل إقباله. وصدق الله تعالى في جميع أحواله، وأنقطع عن هواجس نفسه ولم يصغ بقلبه الى خاطر يدعوه الى غيره فاذا صار من الخلق أجنبيا و من آفات نفسـه بـريـا ومـن المساكنات والملاحظات تقيا ودامت في السر مع الله تعالى مناجاته وحق في كل لحظة إليه رجوعه وصار مُحدثا من براهين اصول المعارف الالهية، ابو طالب التجليل . مطبعة الحوزة العلمية، قم، ١٤١٧هـ، ١٩٩٥م، ج ٢٣ ص ١٠
Ibn Sina says: 'Indeed, the gnostics have stations and degrees with which they are distinguished while in this worldly life, unlike others. It is as if they, while in the garments of their bodies, have stripped them off and disengaged from them to the world of sanctity. They have hidden matters within them and apparent matters from them, denied by those who deny them and deemed great by those who know them.' And according to the expression of Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi: 'When the human spirit disengages from the body, it comes out of its confinement from the house of its mold and the homelands of its nature, emigrating to its Lord to witness His greatest signs. It purifies itself from impurities, pleasures, desires, habitual whisperings, and attachments. Then the light of knowledge, faith, and its highest dominion shines upon it. When this light becomes strong and substantial, it becomes a holy substance called by the philosophers in the language of theoretical wisdom the Active Intellect, and in the language of prophetic law the Holy Spirit. This intense intellectual light radiates the secrets of the earth and heaven, and the realities of things appear from it.' And in the Whispered Prayer of the Gnostics narrated from Imam Ali ibn al-Husayn (peace be upon him): 'O God, make us among those whose trees of longing for You have taken root in the gardens of their breasts, and the burning of Your love has seized the totality of their hearts. They take refuge in the nests of thoughts, graze in the meadows of nearness and unveiling, drink from the watering places of love with the cup of kindness. Their drink is sweet from the spring of dealing, their secret is good in the assembly of intimacy, and their souls are at ease by returning to the Lord of lords, and their eyes are comforted by gazing at their Beloved. O God, how delicious are the inspirations of remembrance of You upon hearts, and how sweet is the journey to You through imaginations in the pathways of the unseen, and how pleasant is the taste of Your love, and how sweet is the drink of Your nearness!' And in the expression of Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (peace be upon him): 'When God Almighty chooses a servant for the affairs of His servants, He expands his breast, deposits in his heart springs of wisdom, and inspires him with knowledge directly, so that he does not become confused by an answer, nor is he perplexed regarding what is correct. He is infallible, supported, successful, guided, safe from sins, slips, and stumbles. God distinguishes him with that to make him His proof upon His servants and His witness over His creation. That is God's bounty; He gives it to whom He wills, and God is the possessor of great bounty.' And according to the expression of Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri (370-475 AH): 'Every knower of God is a gnostic, and every gnostic is a knower. According to these people, gnosis is a quality of one who knows the Truth (God) by His names and attributes, then is truthful with God in his dealings, then his bad morals and afflictions are removed from him, then his standing at the door is prolonged and his retreat in the heart continues, so God Almighty favors him with beautiful acceptance. He is truthful with God in all his states, cuts off from the whisperings of his ego, and does not incline his heart to any thought that calls him to other than Him. When he becomes a stranger to creation, free from the afflictions of his ego, pious from attachments and observations, and his whispered prayers with God continue in secret, and his return to Him is realized at every moment, he becomes one who speaks from proofs of the principles of divine sciences.'
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of authoritative quotes from Islamic philosophers, mystics, and Imams describing the nature of the gnostic (al-'arif) and his knowledge (ma'rifa). The author presents these texts to illustrate the spiritual and epistemological state of those who have attained direct knowledge of God. The passage begins with Ibn Sina, who states that the gnostics have stations and degrees unique to them even in this worldly life. They appear to be in bodies but have effectively stripped them off, disengaging to the world of sanctity. They have hidden inner experiences and outward manifestations that some deny and others acknowledge. Then Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi elaborates: when the human spirit disengages from the body, it escapes its confines and migrates to its Lord. After purification, a light of knowledge and faith shines, which if strengthened becomes a holy substance called the Active Intellect by philosophers and the Holy Spirit by prophets. This intense intellectual light reveals the secrets of the universe and the realities of things. Next, the author quotes the famous supplication of Imam Ali ibn al-Husayn (Zayn al-'Abidin), which poetically describes the gnostic's longing, love, and intimate communion with God. The prayer asks to be among those whose hearts are rooted in longing, consumed by love, and who find peace in returning to God. Following that, Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida describes the servant chosen by God: God expands his chest, fills his heart with wisdom, and inspires him directly, making him infallible in answers and correct in guidance. This special status is God's bounty given to whom He wills. Finally, Abd al-Karim al-Qushayri, a renowned Sufi scholar, defines the gnostic as one who knows God by His names and attributes, is truthful in dealings, purifies his character, and remains constant in devotion. He becomes a stranger to creation, free from ego and attachments, and his constant inner communion with God enables him to speak from the proofs of divine sciences. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that true knowledge is not merely theoretical but involves spiritual transformation and direct experiential awareness of the divine.

Mystical Knowledge: Definitions from Three Authorities
Author collects definitions of 'arif and ma'rifa from three authoritative sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare and contrast the definitions of 'arif and ma'rifa given by Imam al-Rida, al-Qushayri, and al-Kindi, and identify their common emphasis on divine selection and spiritual purification.

Core Idea
Ma'rifa is a divine gift, not a human acquisition.

This lesson presents three authoritative perspectives on mystical knowledge (ma'rifa) and the knower ('arif). Imam al-Rida describes the chosen servant who is divinely inspired and infallible. Al-Qushayri outlines the path of knowing Allah by names and attributes, then purifying oneself. Al-Kindi speaks of the soul's purification leading to visionary knowledge and spiritual pleasure. As you study these definitions, you will learn to distinguish the nuances and shared themes in how Islamic scholars conceptualized direct knowledge of the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources
The passage quotes three authorities: Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida, Abd al-Karim al-Qushayri, and Abu Yusuf al-Kindi. Note their backgrounds: a Shia Imam, a Sufi master, and a philosopher. Their definitions reflect their respective traditions.
2
Extract the essential attributes of the 'arif
From each source, list the key characteristics of the 'arif. For al-Rida: chosen by Allah, expanded breast, inspired knowledge, infallible. For al-Qushayri: knows Allah by names/attributes, truthful, purified, long standing, divine favor. For al-Kindi: purified soul, sees dreams, receives visions, experiences spiritual pleasure.
These attributes are not exhaustive but highlight the core of ma'rifa.
3
Compare the definitions
Compare the three definitions. Ask: What common elements appear? (e.g., divine selection, purification, certainty). What differences exist? (al-Rida emphasizes infallibility, al-Qushayri emphasizes process, al-Kindi emphasizes visionary experience).
Note that al-Qushayri explicitly equates 'alim and 'arif, while the others imply a special status.
4
Synthesize the concept
Combine the insights to form a unified understanding of ma'rifa: it is a direct, divinely granted cognition that comes through purification and divine favor, resulting in certainty and spiritual pleasure, and is distinct from ordinary sensory or intellectual knowledge.
This synthesis serves as a working definition for further study in Islamic epistemology.

And they became tranquil by returning to the Lord of lords, their eyes settled by looking at their beloved, O God, how delicious are the inspirations of Your remembrance upon hearts, and how sweet is the journey to You through imaginations in the pathways of the unseen, and how pleasant is the taste of Your love, and how sweet is the drink of Your nearness. And in the expression of Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida, peace be upon him: 'When Allah, glorified and exalted, chooses a servant for the affairs of His servants, He expands his breast and deposits in his heart the springs of wisdom, and inspires him with knowledge as inspiration. So he is not at a loss for an answer, nor is he confused about the truth. He is infallible, supported, successful, guided, secure from sins, slips, and stumbles. Allah distinguishes him with that to be His proof over His servants and His witness over His creation. That is the bounty of Allah which He gives to whom He wills, and Allah is the possessor of great bounty.' And in the expression of Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri, who is among the early authors (376-475 AH): 'Every scholar of Allah is a knower ('arif) and every knower is a scholar. According to these people, knowledge (ma'rifa) is the attribute of one who knows the Real, glorified, by His names and attributes, then is truthful with Allah in his dealings, then his base morals and defects are removed from him, then his standing at the door (of Allah) becomes long and his seclusion in the heart continues, so Allah favors him with His beautiful acceptance, and he is truthful with Allah in all his states, and he cuts off from the whisperings of his ego, and does not incline his heart to any thought that calls him to other than Him, so that he becomes a stranger to creation, free from the afflictions of his ego, pious from habits and observations, his private supplication with Allah continues, and his return to Him is realized in every moment, and he becomes a speaker about the proofs of the foundations of divine knowledges.' The theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. The Real, glorified, by defining His secrets in what He executes from the measures of His decrees, and one is called at that point a knower ('arif) and his state is called knowledge (ma'rifa). And in the expression of the Islamic philosopher al-Kindi (d. 252 AH): 'When the soul reaches a degree of purity, it sees in sleep wonders of dreams, and souls that have separated from bodies speak to it, and the Creator pours upon it from His light and mercy. So it then experiences a pleasure above the pleasure that comes from food, drink, marriage, hearing, sight, smell, and touch, because these are base sensory pleasures that are followed by harm, while that is a divine, spiritual, heavenly pleasure that is followed by the greatest honor. This soul never sleeps absolutely, because during sleep it leaves the use of the senses, so it knows everything in knowledge, both apparent and hidden. Thus a person reaches knowledge of the secrets of the world, i.e., he witnesses universal realities, in which there is no possibility of error.' Imam Ali, peace be upon him, the master of the knowers, says: 'I never doubted the truth since I was shown it.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of authoritative statements defining mystical knowledge (ma'rifa) and the one who possesses it (al-'arif). The author begins with a poetic invocation expressing yearning for divine intimacy, then presents a hadith-like statement from Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida describing a servant chosen by Allah: his chest is expanded, wisdom springs in his heart, and he is divinely inspired with knowledge, rendering him infallible in answers and firm in truth. This establishes the concept of divinely granted knowledge. Next, al-Qushayri clarifies the relationship between 'alim (scholar) and 'arif (gnostic): every 'alim of Allah is an 'arif, and every 'arif is an 'alim. He then delineates the process of attaining ma'rifa: first knowing Allah by His names and attributes, then being truthful in dealings, then purifying oneself from base morals, then prolonged spiritual effort and seclusion, leading to divine favor and the ability to speak about the proofs of divine knowledges. The author then adds his own definition: the Real defines His secrets through His decrees, and the one who perceives this is called 'arif and his state ma'rifa. Al-Kindi's explanation follows: through extreme purification, the soul in sleep receives visions and knowledge from separated souls and divine light, experiencing a superior spiritual pleasure. This soul never truly sleeps as it gains knowledge of hidden realities beyond error. Finally, Imam Ali's statement underscores the certainty that comes with direct witnessing of truth, eliminating doubt. The passage demonstrates that ma'rifa is a direct, divinely bestowed cognition beyond ordinary intellectual acquisition.

Key Terms
عارف
معرفة
عالم

Quranic Epistemology: Four Stages of Knowledge
Author outlines the Quranic stages of knowledge acquisition and the concept of divine illumination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four Quranic stages of knowledge acquisition: sense, intellect, heart, and divine light, and distinguish between acquired knowledge and granted illumination.

Core Idea
Knowledge in the Quran culminates in divine light, not intellectual achievement.

This passage from a work on Quranic epistemology presents a framework that transcends purely rational philosophy. The author outlines a hierarchy of knowledge acquisition beginning with the senses, rising through intellect and heart, and culminating in a divine light (nur) that Allah bestows upon whomever He wills. This lesson unpacks each stage and its implications for the relationship between scholarship and spiritual insight.

Guided Path
1
List the four stages in order
Identify the sequence given by the author: sense (al-hiss), intellect (al-‘aql), heart (al-qalb), and light (al-nur). Notice that light is the final and highest stage.
2
Understand the function of each stage
Sense provides basic perception; intellect processes sensory data; heart enables deeper understanding; light is divine illumination poured onto the heart. The first three are acquired through ordinary means; the last is a direct gift from Allah.
3
Contrast worldly knowledge and divine light
The author emphasizes that a great scholar or philosopher may be devoid of light, while an ordinary person may overflow with it. This shows that divine illumination is not earned by intellectual effort.
This contrasts with the emphasis in Greek-inspired philosophy on reason alone.
4
Connect the hadith to the framework
The hadith mentions qualities of Allah's friends: trust in Allah and self-sufficiency with Him. These traits likely flow from the light stage, indicating that practical spirituality results from divine illumination.
The hadith is truncated but supports the idea that light leads to exemplary character.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن نظرية المعرفة في القرآن الكريم تحدثت عن مراحل الحس والعقل ثم القلب ثم النور الذي يفيضه الله على قلب الانسان بتدفق غير اعتيادي فمن الممكن ان تجد عالما كبيرا أو فيلسوفا عظيما خاليا من النور وقد تجد رجلا عاديا يفيض بالنور. جاء في الحديث الشريف ( ثلاث صفات من صفة اولياء الله : الثقة بالله في كل شيء ، والغناء به
Indeed, the theory of knowledge in the Noble Quran spoke about the stages of sense, then intellect, then heart, then the light that Allah pours upon the heart of man with an extraordinary flow. So it is possible that you find a great scholar or a great philosopher devoid of light, and you may find an ordinary man overflowing with light. It came in the noble hadith: 'Three qualities from the qualities of the friends of Allah: trust in Allah in everything, and self-sufficiency with Him...'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the Quranic epistemological framework as distinct from purely rational or philosophical approaches. The author identifies four stages of knowledge: first, sensory perception (al-hiss), which is the most basic; second, intellect (al-'aql), which processes sensory data; third, heart (al-qalb), which is the seat of deeper understanding; and finally, light (al-noor), which is a divine effusion upon the heart. This light is not acquired through effort but is a gift from Allah, flowing extraordinarily. The author then makes a crucial point: this divine light is not correlated with worldly scholarship or philosophical brilliance. A great scholar or philosopher may be devoid of this light, while an ordinary person may be filled with it. This underscores the Quran's emphasis on divine guidance over human intellect alone. The passage then cites a hadith listing qualities of Allah's friends, including trust in Allah and self-sufficiency with Him, which are likely fruits of this light. The passage thus contrasts the Quranic epistemology with that of philosophers, who may rely solely on reason and sense, missing the heart and light stages.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic epistemology culminates in divine light (nur), a gift from Allah that is not tied to ordinary learning.

The Nature of Ma'rifa as Divine Light
Author defines gnosis and illuminates its divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ma'rifa as a form of divine illumination distinct from acquired knowledge, using evidence from the passage.

Core Idea
Ma'rifa is a divine light, not mere learning.

The passage opens by defining the 'arif and ma'rifa as a state bestowed by God. Al-Kindi's description of the purified soul receiving knowledge through divine light provides a concrete illustration. This lesson focuses on the concept of ma'rifa as a special gift of divine illumination that transcends ordinary sensory and rational knowledge, but does not negate them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'arif and ma'rifa
Locate the opening sentence where the author says 'the Truth (God) defines His secrets... one is then called an 'arif and his state is called ma'rifa'. Notice that ma'rifa is tied to God's action, not human effort.
2
Analyze al-Kindi's description of the purified soul
Read al-Kindi's quote: when the soul reaches purity, it receives divine light in dreams, gains knowledge beyond the senses, and experiences a superior pleasure. This illustrates ma'rifa as direct divine gift.
Al-Kindi (d. 252 AH) is an Islamic philosopher.
3
Extract the key features of ma'rifa
From the passage, list features: it is a state, involves divine light, yields certainty (no possibility of error), and is above sensory knowledge. Note that it can occur in ordinary people, not just scholars.
4
Contrast ma'rifa with acquired knowledge
The author warns against equating ma'rifa with book learning. Use Imam al-Sadiq's statement: 'Knowledge is not by learning; it is a light that falls into the heart.'
This contrast is crucial.
