The Truth (God) defines His secrets in what He executes of the decrees of His destinies, and one is then called an 'arif and his state is called ma'rifa. And in the statement of the Islamic philosopher al-Kindi (d. 252 AH): 'When the soul reaches a level in purity, it sees in sleep wonders of dreams, and souls that have departed from bodies speak to it, and the Creator pours upon it from His light and mercy, so it then enjoys a pleasure above the pleasure that comes from food, drink, marriage, hearing, sight, smell, and touch, because these are lowly sensory pleasures that bring harm, and that is a divine, spiritual, heavenly pleasure that brings the greatest honor. This soul never sleeps at all, because during sleep it leaves off using the senses, so it knows everything in knowledge and every apparent and hidden thing, and thus man reaches knowledge of the secrets of the world—that is, he witnesses universal realities in which there is no possibility of error.' Imam Ali (peace be upon him), the master of the gnostics, says: 'I have never doubted the truth since it was shown to me.' Indeed, the theory of knowledge in the Noble Quran spoke of the stages of sense, then intellect, then heart, then the light that God pours upon the heart of man in an extraordinary flow. So it is possible that you find a great scholar or a great philosopher devoid of this light, and you may find an ordinary man overflowing with light. It came in the noble hadith: 'Three attributes from the attributes of God's saints: trust in God in everything, self-sufficiency through Him from everything, and need for Him in everything.' It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him): 'God, Blessed and Exalted, has hidden His saint among His servants, so do not belittle any servant of God's servants, for perhaps he is His saint and you do not know.' 'The saints of God are those who looked at the inner reality of this world when people looked at its outward appearance, and busied themselves with its appointed terms when people busied themselves with its immediate affairs. So they caused to die from it what they feared would cause them to die, and they left from it what they knew would leave them.' This is what Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) meant when he addressed 'Unwan al-Basri: 'Knowledge is not by learning; it is a light that falls into the heart of whomever God, Blessed and Exalted, wishes to guide.' And this statement refers to the blessed verse: 'God guides to His light whom He wills.' Some gnostics liken one who follows knowledge through intellect and sense to one who walks on a wooden leg compared to the 'arif, but the reality is that intellect and sense are the means of perception even in extraordinary, illuminative, or divine knowledge. And in the story of Prophet Solomon, peace be upon him, when he wanted to bring the throne of the Queen of Sheba from Yemen to Damascus, there is clarity regarding the extent of the extraordinary ability obtained by one who has a part of the knowledge of the Book. He said: 'He said, O assembly, which of you will bring me her throne before they come to me in submission? A strong one among the jinn said, I will bring it to you before you rise from your place, and I am indeed strong and trustworthy. The one who had knowledge of the Book said, I will bring it to you before your glance returns to you.' And when...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an Islamic epistemological framework that integrates sensory, intellectual, and spiritual dimensions, culminating in the concept of divine illumination. The author begins by defining the 'arif (gnostic) and ma'rifa (gnosis) as a state bestowed by God. The quote from al-Kindi describes the purified soul receiving knowledge through dreams and divine light, gaining certainty. The author then outlines the Quranic stages: sense, intellect, heart, and divine light (noor). This light is not tied to worldly scholarship; ordinary people may possess it. Hadiths from Imam Ali and Imam al-Sadiq emphasize that knowledge is a divine light, not mere learning. Imam al-Sadiq's statement 'laysa al-'ilm bi al-ta'allum innama huwa noor' is a key claim. The author addresses a potential objection by noting that reason and senses remain the means of perception even in extraordinary knowledge. The story of Solomon and Asif ibn Barkhiya (the one with knowledge of the Book) illustrates that such knowledge enables supernatural acts. Throughout, the author argues that true knowledge (ma'rifa) is a gift from God, not solely an acquisition.

The Quranic Epistemology: Sense, Intellect, Heart, and Divine Light
Author presents Quranic stages of knowledge with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four stages of Quranic knowledge acquisition as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
True knowledge integrates sense, intellect, heart, and God's light.

This lesson unpacks the Quranic epistemological framework that the author explicitly outlines: sense, then intellect, then heart, then divine light. The passage emphasizes that this light is not tied to scholarly status and can be found in ordinary people. The story of Prophet Solomon and the one with 'knowledge of the Book' demonstrates the extraordinary power that such knowledge can confer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four stages of knowledge
Locate the sentence: 'the theory of knowledge in the Noble Quran spoke of the stages of sense, then intellect, then heart, then the light that God pours upon the heart.' List them in order.
2
Understand the role of the heart
The heart (qalb) is the spiritual organ that receives divine light. The passage quotes Imam al-Sadiq: 'Knowledge is not by learning; it is a light that falls into the heart.'
The heart is not the physical organ but a spiritual faculty.
3
Analyze the condition for receiving divine light
The light is poured 'in an extraordinary flow' and is not dependent on human effort or scholarship. The author warns not to belittle any servant of God, as the saint may be hidden.
This emphasizes God's will in granting knowledge.
4
Examine the example of Prophet Solomon
The one with 'knowledge of the Book' brought the throne instantly. This illustrates that those who possess divine knowledge can perform extraordinary acts. Relate this to the concept of ma'rifa as powerful, not just theoretical.
Asif ibn Barkhiya is traditionally identified as the one with knowledge of the Book.
5
Synthesize the stages
Note that the author affirms that reason and senses are still the means of perception even for divine knowledge. The stages are not mutually exclusive but build on each other.
The 'wooden leg' analogy is criticized; the author insists on the necessity of reason and senses.

بمن يمشي برجل خشبية اذا ما قيس بالعارف ولكن الواقع ان العقل والحس هما محطة الادراك حتى في المعرفة الخارقة أو الاشراقية أو الالهية. وفي قصة نبي الله سليمان عليه السلام لما أراد جلب عرش الملكة بلقيس من اليمن الى دمشق وضوح في مدى القدرة الخارقة التي يحصل عليها من لديه جزء من علم الكتاب قال تعالى (قَالَ يَا أَيُّهَا الْمَلَأُ أَيُّكُمْ يَأْتِينِي بِعَرشِهَا قَبل أن يَأْتُونِي مُسلِمِينَ . قَالَ عِفْريت من الْجِنِّ أَنَا أَتيك به قبل أن تقوم من مقامكَ وَإِنِّي عَلَيْهِ لَقَوِي أَمِينٌ ، قَالَ الَّذِي عِندَهُ عِلْمٌ مِنَ الْكِتَابِ أَنَا آتِيكَ بِهِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَرتَدَّ إِلَيْكَ طَرْفُكَ فَلَمًا)
The Truth (God) defines His secrets in what He executes of the decrees of His destinies, and one is then called an 'arif and his state is called ma'rifa. And in the statement of the Islamic philosopher al-Kindi (d. 252 AH): 'When the soul reaches a level in purity, it sees in sleep wonders of dreams, and souls that have departed from bodies speak to it, and the Creator pours upon it from His light and mercy, so it then enjoys a pleasure above the pleasure that comes from food, drink, marriage, hearing, sight, smell, and touch, because these are lowly sensory pleasures that bring harm, and that is a divine, spiritual, heavenly pleasure that brings the greatest honor. This soul never sleeps at all, because during sleep it leaves off using the senses, so it knows everything in knowledge and every apparent and hidden thing, and thus man reaches knowledge of the secrets of the world—that is, he witnesses universal realities in which there is no possibility of error.' Imam Ali (peace be upon him), the master of the gnostics, says: 'I have never doubted the truth since it was shown to me.' Indeed, the theory of knowledge in the Noble Quran spoke of the stages of sense, then intellect, then heart, then the light that God pours upon the heart of man in an extraordinary flow. So it is possible that you find a great scholar or a great philosopher devoid of this light, and you may find an ordinary man overflowing with light. It came in the noble hadith: 'Three attributes from the attributes of God's saints: trust in God in everything, self-sufficiency through Him from everything, and need for Him in everything.' It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him): 'God, Blessed and Exalted, has hidden His saint among His servants, so do not belittle any servant of God's servants, for perhaps he is His saint and you do not know.' 'The saints of God are those who looked at the inner reality of this world when people looked at its outward appearance, and busied themselves with its appointed terms when people busied themselves with its immediate affairs. So they caused to die from it what they feared would cause them to die, and they left from it what they knew would leave them.' This is what Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) meant when he addressed 'Unwan al-Basri: 'Knowledge is not by learning; it is a light that falls into the heart of whomever God, Blessed and Exalted, wishes to guide.' And this statement refers to the blessed verse: 'God guides to His light whom He wills.' Some gnostics liken one who follows knowledge through intellect and sense to one who walks on a wooden leg compared to the 'arif, but the reality is that intellect and sense are the means of perception even in extraordinary, illuminative, or divine knowledge. And in the story of Prophet Solomon, peace be upon him, when he wanted to bring the throne of the Queen of Sheba from Yemen to Damascus, there is clarity regarding the extent of the extraordinary ability obtained by one who has a part of the knowledge of the Book. He said: 'He said, O assembly, which of you will bring me her throne before they come to me in submission? A strong one among the jinn said, I will bring it to you before you rise from your place, and I am indeed strong and trustworthy. The one who had knowledge of the Book said, I will bring it to you before your glance returns to you.' And when...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an Islamic epistemological framework that integrates sensory, intellectual, and spiritual dimensions, culminating in the concept of divine illumination. The author begins by defining the 'arif (gnostic) and ma'rifa (gnosis) as a state bestowed by God. The quote from al-Kindi describes the purified soul receiving knowledge through dreams and divine light, gaining certainty. The author then outlines the Quranic stages: sense, intellect, heart, and divine light (noor). This light is not tied to worldly scholarship; ordinary people may possess it. Hadiths from Imam Ali and Imam al-Sadiq emphasize that knowledge is a divine light, not mere learning. Imam al-Sadiq's statement 'laysa al-'ilm bi al-ta'allum innama huwa noor' is a key claim. The author addresses a potential objection by noting that reason and senses remain the means of perception even in extraordinary knowledge. The story of Solomon and Asif ibn Barkhiya (the one with knowledge of the Book) illustrates that such knowledge enables supernatural acts. Throughout, the author argues that true knowledge (ma'rifa) is a gift from God, not solely an acquisition.

The Quranic Theory of Knowledge: The Role of Baṣā’ir
Author presents four points on the Quranic epistemology: senses, reason, inspiration, and inner perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of baṣā’ir in the Quranic epistemological framework and evaluate how they serve as criteria for truth.

Core Idea
The baṣā’ir are divinely inspired self-evident truths forming the ultimate criterion for all knowledge.

The Quran presents a comprehensive theory of knowledge that integrates sensory experience, rational reflection, and divine inspiration. In this passage, the author summarizes four key components: sensory knowledge, intellect, inspiration, and the baṣā’ir (insights). These insights are the foundation for distinguishing truth from falsehood. Let us unpack how they function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four sources of knowledge
The passage lists sensory knowledge, the intellect, inspiration (ilhām), and insights (baṣā’ir). Note that inspiration is the process that delivers the baṣā’ir.
2
Understand the limitation of sensory knowledge
Sensory knowledge is described as superficial (ẓāhirī) but it awakens the intuitive knowledge (al-‘ilm al-wijdānī) present in the intellect.
3
Grasp the role of baṣā’ir as self-evident propositions
The baṣā’ir are defined as a collection of self-evident propositions (qaḍāyā badīhiyya) that require no proof and are found through inspiration.
4
Apply the test of baṣā’ir
When a proposition is encountered, one refers it to the baṣā’ir. If it agrees, it is true; if not, it is false. This mirrors the role of axioms in logic.
5
Contrast with pure rationalism
Baṣā’ir are not merely innate ideas but are inspired by God, combining reason with revelation. This distinguishes the Islamic epistemology from purely rationalist accounts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصة نظرية القرآن في المعرفة ۱) إن المعرفة الحسية لها أهمية بالغة في المعرفة، ولكنها معرفة سطحية ظاهرية. فالمعرفة الحسية تعمل على تفتح العلم الوجداني الموجود في العقل وتزهره وتطوره فتقدم المعرفة خطوة جوهرية الى الامام ولكن المعرفة الحقيقية الرصينة هي التي تقوم على اساس التعقل والتدبر والتعاطي اليقيني مع المعارف وهو ما يقوم به العقل . ۲ ) العقل ينظم الانطباعات الحسية الخام ويغربلها ويصنفها من خلال قياسها بالبصائر الثابتة التي الهم بها يساهم الالهام الذي هو عبارة عن القاء القوانين الوجدانية في النفس من دون وساطة وهو على قسمين عام الجميع البشر و خاص لفئة معينة منه ٤ ) البصائر : هي علة المعقولات وتعد المعيار والحجر الاساس لتشخيص الحق عن الباطل والصواب عن الخطأ. وهي مجموعة عظيمة من القضايا البدهية يجدها الانسان في باطن عقله من طريق الالهام ويذعن بها من دون حاجة الى إقامة برهان أو يطلب دليل. وهذه البصائر يجهز بها جميع البشر . فاذا طرحت امام الانسان قضية ما لايدري هل هي صحيحة وصادقة أو زائفة وموهومة يرجعها الى القضايا والمعارف البدهية - البصائر - فإن صدقت تلك كانت هذه قضية حقة وان لم تصدقها كانت قضية باطلة
Summary of the Qur'anic theory of knowledge: 1) Sensory knowledge has great importance in knowledge, but it is superficial, apparent knowledge. Sensory knowledge works to open up the intuitive knowledge (al-‘ilm al-wijdānī) present in the intellect, to blossom it and develop it, thus advancing knowledge a substantial step forward. But real, solid knowledge is that which is based on reasoning (ta‘aqqul), contemplation (tadabbur), and certain engagement with knowledges, and that is what the intellect does. 2) The intellect organizes raw sensory impressions, sifts and classifies them through measuring them with the fixed insights (baṣā'ir) with which it is inspired. 3) Inspiration (ilhām) contributes, which is the casting of intuitive laws into the soul without mediation, and it is of two types: general for all human beings, and specific for a particular group of them. 4) Insights (baṣā'ir): They are the cause of intelligibles and are the criterion and foundation for distinguishing truth from falsehood and right from wrong. They are a great collection of self-evident propositions that a person finds in the depths of his intellect through the way of inspiration and assents to them without need to establish a proof or seek evidence. These insights are provided to all human beings. So when a certain proposition is presented to a person, and he does not know whether it is true and veracious or false and illusory, he refers it to the self-evident propositions and knowledge (the insights). If it accords with them, then it is a true proposition; if it does not accord with them, it is a false proposition.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive Quranic theory of knowledge in four points. The first point acknowledges the importance of sensory knowledge but notes its superficiality; it serves to awaken the intuitive knowledge (al-‘ilm al-wijdānī) latent in the intellect. True knowledge is based on reasoning (ta‘aqqul) and certain engagement. The second point describes the intellect's role in organizing sensory data through fixed insights (baṣā’ir). The third point introduces inspiration (ilhām) as the direct casting of intuitive laws into the soul, divided into general (for all humans) and specific (for a chosen group). The fourth point defines baṣā’ir as self-evident propositions (qaḍāyā badīhiyya) that are the ultimate criteria for truth. They are innate, found through inspiration, and require no proof. Any claim is evaluated by its agreement with these insights. This mirrors the role of axioms in logic; for example, the principle that injustice is bad is self-evident. The theory integrates empirical and rational elements within an Islamic framework.

Key Terms
العلم الوجداني
البصائر
القضايا البدهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) They are legal rulings
D) They are special dreams

Key Takeaway
The Quranic theory of knowledge places baṣā’ir as divinely inspired, self-evident foundations for all knowledge, against which all claims are measured.

The Quranic Theory of Knowledge: Sources and Decision
Author outlines sources of knowledge and decision process.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sources of knowledge mentioned in the passage and describe the role of the balance (mizan) in the soul's decision-making process.

Core Idea
Knowledge is multi-sourced, processed by a divinely ordained balance for sound decisions.

This passage presents the Quranic view on how we acquire knowledge and make decisions. The soul collects information from diverse sources — sensory, rational, spiritual, and revealed — then processes it through a metaphorical 'balance' (mizan) to reach a decision. Understanding this framework helps us appreciate how Islamic epistemology integrates revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sources of knowledge
Locate the list of sources in the Arabic text: الحس، العقل، الفطرة، البصائر، الوحي، الإلهام، الرؤيا المنامية، القلب، الصدر، الكتب السماوية، الأنبياء، التجارب البشرية. Note the variety: sensory, rational, innate, inspirational, revelatory, and experiential.
These correspond to different avenues of knowledge in Islamic thought.
2
Trace the information flow
The soul sends information to the memory (الذاكرة), which then forwards it to the balance (الميزان). Understand that memory is not the final processor but a relay.
3
Understand the mizan's function
The balance 'presents a concentrated summary' (يقدم خلاصة مركزة). It is a evaluative faculty that synthesizes and weighs information to aid decision-making.
In later Islamic philosophy, mizan often means logic; here it is a broader criterion.
4
Describe the decision outcome
The soul makes a decision based on the summary from the mizan, and the body executes it. The nature of the decision is subject to all the factors listed.
This shows that Islamic epistemology is not purely rational; it includes spiritual and experiential elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن النفس تتسلم المعلومات من مختلف مصادرها : الحس ، العقل ، الفطرة ، البصائر ، الوحي ، الالهام ، الرؤيا المنامية ، القلب ، الصدر ، الكتب السماوية والانبياء والتجارب البشرية فترسلها الى الذاكرة التي ترسل المعلومات بدورها الى الميزان الذي يقدم خلاصة مركزة على ضوئها تتخذ النفس قرارها الذي ينفذه الجسد إن طبيعة القرار الذي تتخذه النفس يخضع لهذه العوامل جميعا وهذه خلاصة نظرية القرآن في المعرفة
Indeed, the soul receives information from its various sources: the senses, the intellect, the innate disposition, insights, revelation, inspiration, dream visions, the heart, the chest, the heavenly books, the prophets, and human experiences. It sends them to the memory, which in turn sends the information to the balance that presents a concentrated summary. In the light of this, the soul makes its decision, which the body executes. Indeed, the nature of the decision that the soul makes is subject to all these factors. And this is the summary of the Quranic theory of knowledge.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the Quranic theory of knowledge. It begins by enumerating the sources from which the soul (nafs) acquires information: sensory perception ('hiss), reason ('aql), innate disposition (fitra), inner insights (basa'ir), revelation (wahy), inspiration (ilham), dreams (ru'ya manamiyya), heart (qalb), chest (sadr), heavenly books, prophets, and human experiences. The soul then sends this information to the memory (dhakira), which forwards it to the 'balance' (mizan). The mizan is a metaphorical criterion or evaluative faculty that produces a concentrated summary, upon which the soul bases its decision. The body then executes this decision. The author emphasizes that the nature of the decision is influenced by all these factors collectively, concluding that this constitutes the essence of the Quranic epistemology.

Distinguishing Quranic 'Basa'ir' from Philosophical Primary Principles
Author contrasts Quranic 'basa'ir' with philosophical innate ideas and primary principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic concept of 'basa'ir' from the theories of innate ideas and primary principles, and explain its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Quranic 'basa'ir' are divinely bestowed insights, not merely innate rational principles.

This passage draws attention to a fundamental epistemological contrast: while Descartes and others place foundational knowledge in the mind's innate structure, the Quran attributes it to divine gift. Understanding this difference is crucial for appreciating the Quranic epistemology that distinguishes knowledge from mere rationality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three theories
Find in the Arabic passage the three terms: 'innate ideas' (Descartes), 'primary principles' (latent power theory), and 'basa'ir' (Quran). Note the exact phrasing used for each.
2
Contrast the terminology
Notice the shift from 'afkar fitriyya' and 'mabadi' uwaliyya' to 'basa'ir'. What does 'basa'ir' connotate that the others do not? Consider its root meaning of inner sight or discernment.
3
Probe the epistemological basis
The passage suggests that the source of these principles differs: for Descartes, they are inherent; for the Quran, they are given. How does this affect the validation of knowledge? Does it imply revelation is necessary for certainty?
4
Assess implications for Islamic thought
If knowledge ultimately comes from divine insight, what role does reason play? This challenges the autonomy of reason as the sole foundation. Mutakallimun often integrate reason with revelation; this contrast clarifies the debate.
See also al-Ghazali's critique of the philosophers in Tahafut al-Falasifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸ ) ويوجد اختلاف حول المبادىء العقلية الاولية بين القرآن الكريم ونظريتي الافكار الفطرية والقوة الكامنة ففي حين سمتها نظرية ديكارت افكارا فطرية واسمتها نظرية القوة الكامنة المباديء الاولية سمتها نظرية القرآن بصائر.
8) There is a difference regarding the primary rational principles between the Noble Quran and the two theories of innate ideas and latent power: whereas Descartes' theory called them innate ideas and the theory of latent power called them primary principles, the Quran's theory called them insights (basa'ir).

Commentary
This passage identifies a key epistemological difference: the Quran employs the term 'basa'ir' (insights) for foundational knowledge, contrasting with Descartes' 'innate ideas' and the theory of latent power's 'primary principles'. The term 'basa'ir' implies a divinely granted clarity, not merely inherent rationality. This distinction affects how knowledge is validated—through revelation and innate guidance rather than pure reason.

The Quranic Centrality of Emotional Knowledge vs. Philosophical Rationalism
Contrasts Quranic vs. philosophical views on emotional knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Quranic and philosophical approaches to emotional knowledge and identify the implications for Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
The Quran centers emotional knowledge in the heart, while philosophy marginalizes emotions.

This passage draws a sharp contrast between the Quranic and philosophical treatments of emotions. Philosophers often treat the mind as a purely computational faculty, relegating feelings to the periphery. The Quran, by contrast, gives emotional knowledge a central role, designating the heart as its seat. Understanding this difference is crucial for grasping the Islamic epistemological framework, where affections are not opposed to reason but integrated with it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two approaches
Notice that the Quran gives a 'specialized central position' to emotional knowledge, while philosophers treat emotions as 'marginal and fluctuating'.
2
Understand the Quranic term 'heart' (qalb)
It is a metaphor for conscience and a cognitive-emotional faculty. It is not just a synonym for 'mind' or 'soul'.
Al-qalb in Islamic psychology is also the spiritual heart.
3
Examine the philosophical analogy
The mind is compared to a computer that processes inputs and outputs without feelings. This reveals a purely rationalist view.
4
Consider the epistemological implication
For the Quran, emotions are part of knowing and can lead to action; for philosophers, emotions are separate and often devalued.

Quranic Epistemology: Hearing Over Sight
Author compares Quranic privileging of hearing over sight with philosophical equal treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic hierarchy of the senses (hearing > sight) and contrast it with the philosophical theory of latent power.

Core Idea
In the Quran, hearing ranks above sight, reversing the philosophical order.

This passage highlights a key epistemological distinction: the Quran gives special preference to hearing and sight among the senses, with hearing taking the highest rank. This contrasts with philosophical theories that treat all senses equally or favor sight. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for grasping how revelation (hearing) is prioritized over observation (sight) in Islamic knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
Find the Quranic view: hearing and sight are privileged, with hearing superior. Then find the philosophical view: senses are equal or sight is preferred.
2
Locate the term 'latent power theory'
Recognize that the 'theory of latent power' represents a philosophical stance that favors sight over hearing, in direct opposition to the Quran.
The author does not define this theory further; it may refer to certain empiricist or rationalist views.
3
Analyze the conflict
Note the explicit conflict: the Quran prefers hearing, the latent power theory prefers sight. This is not just a difference but a contradiction.
4
Relate to Islamic epistemology
Connect hearing to revelation (oral, aural) and sight to observation. The Quran prioritizes revelation as the primary source of knowledge.
This hierarchy influences how scholars weigh evidence: transmitted report (khabar) takes precedence over empirical reasoning in certain contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage notes that the Quran distinctly favors hearing and sight among the senses, while granting hearing a higher rank than sight. This opposes the latent power theory (likely referring to certain empiricist views) that prioritized vision. The Quranic emphasis on hearing aligns with its oral-aural tradition (revelation via recitation), while sight is also valued but secondary. This hierarchical ordering influences how knowledge is acquired—through listening to revelation and observing creation, but the former is primary.

Stages of Knowledge: Quran vs Philosophy
Author contrasts Quran's multiple stages with philosophical unitary stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic claim that knowledge has multiple stages requiring reflection, contrasting with the philosophical claim of a single stage.

Core Idea
The Quran sees knowledge as a journey; philosophy sees it as a single step.

The passage presents a fundamental contrast: while most philosophical theories assert that the stage of knowledge is singular, the Quran insists on multiple stages and calls for prolonged reflection. This distinction shapes how certainty (yaqin) is achieved in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim
Find in the passage the statement that most philosophical theories said the stage of knowledge is one. What does 'one stage' imply about how knowledge is gained?
2
Identify the Quranic counterclaim
Locate the Quranic position that knowledge has multiple stages and requires reflection and long thinking to reach certain knowledge. Why might multiple stages be necessary?
3
Understand the goal of certainty
The passage mentions 'reach certain knowledge' (al-ma'rifa al-yaqiniyya). Reflect on how multiple stages contribute to certainty versus a single-stage view.
4
Apply to Islamic theology
Consider how this multiple-stage view impacts theological proofs, such as proving God's existence. Does it require moving through doubt, evidence, and reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۳ ) ان معظم نظريات الفلسفة قالت ان مرحلة المعرفة واحدة في حين يقول القرآن الكريم ان للمعرفة مراحل متعددة ودعا الى التفكر والتفكير الطويل للوصول الى المعرفة اليقينية . ١٤ ) قالت اغلب نظريات الفلسفة ان علوم الانسان كلها مكتسبة حصولية لجميع الناس في حين قالت نظرية القرآن الكريم ان علوم الانسان تنقسم على قسمين هما أ - ثابتة حضورية موجودة عند جميع الناس بالتساوي مثل البصائر ب - حية متنوعة حصولية تختلف من انسان لآخر مثل الوحي للانبياء والاصطفاء للاولياء والرؤيا المنامية لبعض الصالحين
13) Most philosophical theories said that the stage of knowledge is one, whereas the Noble Quran says that knowledge has multiple stages and calls for reflection and long thinking to reach certain knowledge. 14) Most philosophical theories said that all human sciences are acquired (husuli) for all people, whereas the Quranic theory said that human sciences are divided into two types: A) Constant presential (huduri) knowledge present in all people equally, such as insights (basa'ir); B) Living varied acquired knowledge that differs from one person to another, such as revelation for prophets, selection for saints, and dream visions for some righteous people.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasts: First, the Quran posits multiple stages of knowledge requiring deep reflection to reach certainty, against philosophical monism of a single stage. Second, the Quran classifies knowledge into presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli), with presential being constant and equal for all (like the innate insights), while acquired is varied (prophetic revelation, saintly inspiration, dreams). This division highlights the Quran's recognition of both common innate guidance and special divine gifts.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's multiple-stage view emphasizes the necessity of active reflection and prolonged thinking to attain certainty.

Types of Knowledge: Presential and Acquired
Author contrasts Quran's two types of knowledge with philosophical wholly acquired view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli) knowledge according to the Quranic theory, and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Some knowledge is innate and equal; some is gifted and varies.

The passage presents a second contrast: philosophical theories claim all human knowledge is acquired (husuli), while the Quran divides knowledge into two categories: constant presential knowledge present equally in all people (like insights) and living varied acquired knowledge that differs among individuals (like revelation, inspiration, dreams). This classification is crucial for understanding how divine guidance works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim
Find the statement that most philosophical theories said all human sciences are acquired for all people. What does 'acquired' mean in this context?
2
Identify the Quranic classification
Locate the two types of knowledge: constant presential (huduri) and living varied acquired (husuli). Note the Arabic terms.
3
Understand presential knowledge
Presential knowledge is described as constant and present equally in all people. The example given is 'basa'ir' (insights). Think of basic moral or rational intuitions.
4
Understand acquired knowledge
Acquired knowledge varies between individuals. The passage lists: revelation for prophets, selection (istifa') for saints, and dream visions (ru'ya manamiyya) for some righteous people.
5
Reflect on the epistemological significance
Why does the Quran make this distinction? It shows that while all humans share foundational knowledge, special divine gifts elevate certain individuals.

Divine Effusion as Foundation of Knowledge
Author critiques empiricist epistemology by grounding it in divine effusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic critique of empiricism regarding the foundation of knowledge.

Core Idea
Experience alone is insufficient; divine effusion roots all knowledge and gives it value.

The author contrasts empiricist theories (Locke, Marx, Hume) with the Quranic view. For empiricists, experience is the sole path. The Quran teaches that divine effusion (al-fayd al-ilahi) provides primary knowledges, making experience meaningful and revealing more than surface phenomena.

The passage says empiricists only perceive the apparent of worldly life.

Integration of Ethics into Quranic Epistemology
Author contrasts philosophical focus on cognitive mechanics with Quranic ethical integration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate how the Quranic theory of knowledge integrates ethics with epistemology.

Core Idea
Quranic knowledge is inseparable from ethical calling.

The author notes that most philosophical theories focus on the mechanism of perception (how decisions arise). In contrast, the Quranic theory of knowledge is intertwined with a call to practice good and abandon evil. This shows that knowledge in Islam is not value-neutral.

Knowledge is not merely cognitive but has moral purpose.

Quranic vs Philosophical Concept of Intellect
Author compares Quranic and philosophical definitions of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic concept of 'aql from the philosophical concept as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Quranic 'aql is purposive (truth and justice), not merely cognitive.

The passage contrasts two views of intellect. In the Quran, 'aql (understanding) is a tool for reaching truth (haqq) and justice ('adl). Philosophers treat it as a tool for knowledge and perception alone. This distinction has profound implications for how we value reason.

Guided Path
1
Extract Quranic definition of 'aql
In the Quran's perspective, 'aql means understanding (tafahhum) and is the tool to reach truth and justice.
2
Extract philosophers' definition
In the philosophers' perspective, 'aql is a tool for knowledge and perception.
3
Compare the ends each serves
Quranic 'aql has a moral telos (truth and justice), while philosophical 'aql is merely cognitive.
4
Discuss why the ends matter
The purpose of reason shapes how it is used and valued.

(۱۷) اختلف مفهوم العقل بين القرآن والفلاسفة فالعقل في منظور القرآن بمعنى التفهم وهو اداة الوصول الى الحق والعدل في حين ان العقل في منظور الفلاسفة اداة للمعرفة والادراك . ۱۸ ) اختلف القرآن الكريم مع النظرية الجدلية والنظرية الحسية حول قيمة الواقع الموضوعي فقد قرر القرآن هذا الواقع موضوعا للنظر العقلي لاثبات
15) The theories of Locke, Marx, and Hume said that experience is the essential path to reach knowledge, whereas the Quranic theory said that the divine effusion which gave primary knowledges is the foundation that gives value to experiences. And experience reflects the phenomena of things; thus the empiricists perceive only the apparent of the worldly life. 16) Most philosophical theories focused on the mechanism of the birth of the perceptual decision, whereas we find that the Quranic theory of knowledge came mixed in all its branches and details with the call to practice good and virtue and abandon evil and vice. 17) The concept of the intellect differed between the Quran and philosophers: the intellect in the Quran's perspective means understanding, and it is the tool to reach truth and justice, whereas the intellect in the philosophers' perspective is a tool for knowledge and perception. 18) The Noble Quran differed with the dialectical theory and the sensory theory regarding the value of objective reality, for the Quran established this reality as a subject for rational reflection to prove...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage covers three final contrasts. First (15): Empiricists (Locke, Marx, Hume) consider experience essential, but the Quran grounds experience in divine effusion, making it secondary; empiricists only grasp surface phenomena. Second (16): Philosophical theories focus on cognitive mechanics, while the Quran integrates ethical calls into all knowledge. Third (17): 'Aql in the Quran is understanding oriented toward truth and justice, whereas philosophers see it as mere cognition. Fourth (18, incomplete): The Quran affirms objective reality as the subject of rational inquiry, contra dialectical and sensory theories that may deny or reduce it. The passage critiques philosophical reductions and affirms a theistic, value-laden epistemology.

Key Terms
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Quranic definition of 'aql?
A) Tool for knowledge and perception
B) Understanding and tool to reach truth and justice
C) Mechanism for perceptual decisions
D) Foundation of experience

Key Takeaway
The Quran redefines 'aql as understanding oriented to truth and justice, not just perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about the role of reason in Islamic law, which definition of 'aql would support using reason to derive rulings that align with justice and truth?
